         Finland is in
         <br/>
         the
         <a href="/wiki/Eurozone" title="Eurozone">
          Eurozone
         </a>
         <br/>
         the
         <a href="/wiki/Schengen_Area" title="Schengen Area">
          Schengen Area
         </a>
         <br/>
         the
         <a href="/wiki/European_Single_Market" title="European Single Market">
          European Single Market
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       <b>
        Finland
       </b>
       (
       <span class="nowrap">
        <span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt">
         <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">
          /
          <span style="border-bottom:1px dotted">
           <span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">
            ˈ
           </span>
           <span title="'f' in 'find'">
            f
           </span>
           <span title="/ɪ/: 'i' in 'kit'">
            ɪ
           </span>
           <span title="'n' in 'nigh'">
            n
           </span>
           <span title="'l' in 'lie'">
            l
           </span>
           <span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">
            ə
           </span>
           <span title="'n' in 'nigh'">
            n
           </span>
           <span title="'d' in 'dye'">
            d
           </span>
          </span>
          /
         </a>
        </span>
        <small class="nowrap">
         (
         <span class="unicode haudio">
          <span class="fn">
           <span style="white-space:nowrap">
            <a href="/wiki/File:En-us-Finland.ogg" title="About this sound">
             <img alt="About this sound" data-file-height="20" data-file-width="20" height="11" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" width="11"/>
            </a>
           </span>
           <a class="internal" href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/b3/En-us-Finland.ogg" title="En-us-Finland.ogg">
            listen
           </a>
          </span>
         </span>
         )
        </small>
       </span>
       ;
       <a href="/wiki/Finnish_language" title="Finnish language">
        Finnish
       </a>
       :
       <i>
        <span lang="fi">
         Suomi
        </span>
       </i>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Estonian_and_Finnish" title="Help:IPA/Estonian and Finnish">
         [suo̯mi]
        </a>
       </span>
       <small class="nowrap">
        (
        <span class="unicode haudio">
         <span class="fn">
          <span style="white-space:nowrap">
           <a href="/wiki/File:Suomi_Finland.ogg" title="About this sound">
            <img alt="About this sound" data-file-height="20" data-file-width="20" height="11" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" width="11"/>
           </a>
          </span>
          <a class="internal" href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/ed/Suomi_Finland.ogg" title="Suomi Finland.ogg">
           listen
          </a>
         </span>
        </span>
        )
       </small>
       ;
       <a href="/wiki/Swedish_language" title="Swedish language">
        Swedish
       </a>
       :
       <i>
        <span lang="sv">
         Finland
        </span>
       </i>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Swedish" title="Help:IPA/Swedish">
         [ˈfɪnland]
        </a>
       </span>
       ), officially the
       <b>
        Republic of Finland
       </b>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Finnish_language" title="Finnish language">
        Finnish
       </a>
       :
       <i>
        <span lang="fi">
         Suomen tasavalta
        </span>
       </i>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Swedish_language" title="Swedish language">
        Swedish
       </a>
       :
       <i>
        <span lang="sv">
         Republiken Finland
        </span>
       </i>
       )
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7">
        <a href="#cite_note-7">
         [7]
        </a>
       </sup>
       is a
       <a href="/wiki/Sovereign_state" title="Sovereign state">
        sovereign state
       </a>
       in
       <a href="/wiki/Northern_Europe" title="Northern Europe">
        Northern Europe
       </a>
       . The country has land borders with
       <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">
        Sweden
       </a>
       to the northwest,
       <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">
        Norway
       </a>
       to the north, and
       <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">
        Russia
       </a>
       to the east. To the south is the
       <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Finland" title="Gulf of Finland">
        Gulf of Finland
       </a>
       with
       <a href="/wiki/Estonia" title="Estonia">
        Estonia
       </a>
       on the opposite side. Finland is a
       <a href="/wiki/Nordic_countries" title="Nordic countries">
        Nordic country
       </a>
       and, together with
       <a href="/wiki/Scandinavia" title="Scandinavia">
        Scandinavia
       </a>
       , is situated in the geographical region of
       <a href="/wiki/Fennoscandia" title="Fennoscandia">
        Fennoscandia
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Finland's population is 5.5 million (2016), and the majority of the population is concentrated in the southern region.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pop_stat_8-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-pop_stat-8">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
       88.7% of the population is
       <a href="/wiki/Finns" title="Finns">
        Finnish
       </a>
       and speaks
       <a href="/wiki/Finnish_language" title="Finnish language">
        Finnish
       </a>
       , a
       <a href="/wiki/Uralic_languages" title="Uralic languages">
        Uralic language
       </a>
       unrelated to the Scandinavian languages; next come the
       <a href="/wiki/Swedish-speaking_population_of_Finland" title="Swedish-speaking population of Finland">
        Finland-Swedes
       </a>
       (5.3%). Finland is the
       <a href="/wiki/Area_and_population_of_European_countries" title="Area and population of European countries">
        eighth-largest country in Europe
       </a>
       and the most
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_countries_and_territories_by_population_density" title="List of countries and territories by population density">
        sparsely populated
       </a>
       country in the
       <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">
        European Union
       </a>
       . It is a
       <a href="/wiki/Parliamentary_system" title="Parliamentary system">
        parliamentary
       </a>
       republic with a central government based in the capital city of
       <a href="/wiki/Helsinki" title="Helsinki">
        Helsinki
       </a>
       , local governments in 311
       <a href="/wiki/Municipalities_of_Finland" title="Municipalities of Finland">
        municipalities
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Suomen_Kuntaliitto_9-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Suomen_Kuntaliitto-9">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and one
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Autonomous_area" title="Autonomous area">
        autonomous region
       </a>
       , the
       <a href="/wiki/%C3%85land_Islands" title="Åland Islands">
        Åland Islands
       </a>
       . Over 1.4 million people live in the
       <a href="/wiki/Greater_Helsinki" title="Greater Helsinki">
        Greater Helsinki
       </a>
       metropolitan area, which produces one third of the country's
       <a href="/wiki/Gross_domestic_product" title="Gross domestic product">
        GDP
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Finland was inhabited when the last
       <a href="/wiki/Ice_age" title="Ice age">
        ice age
       </a>
       ended, approximately 9000 BCE.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10">
        <a href="#cite_note-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The first settlers left behind artifacts that present characteristics shared with those found in Estonia, Russia, and Norway.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_11-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-:1-11">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The earliest people were
       <a href="/wiki/Hunter-gatherer" title="Hunter-gatherer">
        hunter-gatherers
       </a>
       , using stone tools.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VF-Pre_12-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-VF-Pre-12">
         [12]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The first pottery appeared in 5200 BCE, when the
       <a href="/wiki/Pit%E2%80%93Comb_Ware_culture" title="Pit–Comb Ware culture">
        Comb Ceramic culture
       </a>
       was introduced.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hist-Fin-Geo_13-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Hist-Fin-Geo-13">
         [13]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The arrival of the
       <a href="/wiki/Corded_Ware_culture" title="Corded Ware culture">
        Corded Ware culture
       </a>
       in southern coastal Finland between 3000 and 2500 BCE may have coincided with the start of agriculture.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Virt-Mino_14-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Virt-Mino-14">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">
        Bronze Age
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Iron_Age" title="Iron Age">
        Iron Age
       </a>
       were characterised by extensive contacts with other cultures in the Fennoscandian and
       <a href="/wiki/Baltic_region" title="Baltic region">
        Baltic regions
       </a>
       and the sedentary farming inhabitation increased towards the end of
       <a href="/wiki/Iron_Age" title="Iron Age">
        Iron Age
       </a>
       . At the time Finland had three main cultural areas,
       <a href="/wiki/Southwest_Finland" title="Southwest Finland">
        Southwest Finland
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Tavastia_(historical_province)" title="Tavastia (historical province)">
        Tavastia
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Karelia_(historical_province_of_Finland)" title="Karelia (historical province of Finland)">
        Karelia
       </a>
       , as reflected in contemporary jewellery.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15">
        <a href="#cite_note-15">
         [15]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       From the late 13th century, Finland gradually became an integral part of Sweden through the
       <a href="/wiki/Northern_Crusades" title="Northern Crusades">
        crusades
       </a>
       and the Swedish part-colonisation of coastal Finland, a legacy reflected in the prevalence of the Swedish language and its official status. In 1809, Finland was incorporated into the
       <a href="/wiki/Russian_Empire" title="Russian Empire">
        Russian Empire
       </a>
       as the autonomous
       <a href="/wiki/Grand_Duchy_of_Finland" title="Grand Duchy of Finland">
        Grand Duchy of Finland
       </a>
       . In 1906, Finland became the first European state to grant all adult citizens the right to vote, and the first in the world to give all adult citizens the right to run for public office.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eduskunta_16-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-eduskunta-16">
         [16]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-full_suffrage_17-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-full_suffrage-17">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Following the 1917
       <a href="/wiki/Russian_Revolution" title="Russian Revolution">
        Russian Revolution
       </a>
       , Finland declared itself independent. In 1918, the fledgling state was divided by
       <a href="/wiki/Finnish_Civil_War" title="Finnish Civil War">
        civil war
       </a>
       , with the Bolshevik-leaning
       <a href="/wiki/Finnish_Socialist_Workers%27_Republic" title="Finnish Socialist Workers' Republic">
        Red Guard
       </a>
       supported by the equally new
       <a href="/wiki/Russian_Soviet_Federative_Socialist_Republic" title="Russian Soviet Federative Socialist Republic">
        Soviet Russia
       </a>
       , fighting the
       <a href="/wiki/Whites_(Finland)" title="Whites (Finland)">
        White Guard
       </a>
       , supported by the
       <a href="/wiki/German_Empire" title="German Empire">
        German Empire
       </a>
       . After a brief attempt to
       <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Finland_(1918)" title="Kingdom of Finland (1918)">
        establish a kingdom
       </a>
       , the country became a republic. During
       <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">
        World War II
       </a>
       , the Soviet Union sought repeatedly to occupy Finland, with Finland losing parts of
       <a href="/wiki/Karelia" title="Karelia">
        Karelia
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Salla" title="Salla">
        Salla
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Kuusamo" title="Kuusamo">
        Kuusamo
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Petsamo_Province" title="Petsamo Province">
        Petsamo
       </a>
       and some islands, but retaining independence.
      </p>
      <p>
       Finland joined the
       <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">
        United Nations
       </a>
       in 1955 and established an official policy of neutrality. The
       <a href="/wiki/Finno-Soviet_Treaty_of_1948" title="Finno-Soviet Treaty of 1948">
        Finno-Soviet Treaty of 1948
       </a>
       gave the Soviet Union some leverage in Finnish domestic politics during the
       <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">
        Cold War
       </a>
       era. Finland joined the
       <a href="/wiki/OECD" title="OECD">
        OECD
       </a>
       in 1969, the
       <a href="/wiki/Partnership_for_Peace" title="Partnership for Peace">
        NATO Partnership for Peace
       </a>
       in 1994,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nato.int_18-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-nato.int-18">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
       the
       <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">
        European Union
       </a>
       in 1995, the
       <a href="/wiki/Euro-Atlantic_Partnership_Council" title="Euro-Atlantic Partnership Council">
        Euro-Atlantic Partnership Council
       </a>
       in 1997,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nato.int_18-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-nato.int-18">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and finally the
       <a href="/wiki/Eurozone" title="Eurozone">
        Eurozone
       </a>
       at its inception, in 1999.
      </p>
      <p>
       Finland was a relative latecomer to industrialisation, remaining a largely
       <a href="/wiki/Agriculture" title="Agriculture">
        agrarian
       </a>
       country until the 1950s. After
       <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">
        World War II
       </a>
       , the Soviet Union demanded war reparations from Finland not only in money but also in material, such as ships and machinery. This forced Finland to industrialise. It rapidly developed an advanced economy while building an extensive
       <a href="/wiki/Welfare_state" title="Welfare state">
        welfare state
       </a>
       based on the
       <a href="/wiki/Nordic_model" title="Nordic model">
        Nordic model
       </a>
       , resulting in widespread prosperity and one of the highest
       <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_GDP_(nominal)_per_capita" title="List of countries by GDP (nominal) per capita">
        per capita incomes
       </a>
       in the world.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-imf2_19-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-imf2-19">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Finland is a top performer in numerous metrics of national performance, including education, economic competitiveness, civil liberties, quality of life, and human development.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-World_Audit_20-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-World_Audit-20">
         [20]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21">
        <a href="#cite_note-21">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22">
        <a href="#cite_note-22">
         [22]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23">
        <a href="#cite_note-23">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In 2015, Finland was ranked first in the World Human Capital
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24">
        <a href="#cite_note-24">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and the
       <a href="/wiki/Press_Freedom_Index" title="Press Freedom Index">
        Press Freedom Index
       </a>
       and as the most stable country in the world during 2011–2016 in the
       <a href="/wiki/Fragile_States_Index" title="Fragile States Index">
        Fragile States Index
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25">
        <a href="#cite_note-25">
         [25]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and second in the
       <a href="/wiki/Global_Gender_Gap_Report" title="Global Gender Gap Report">
        Global Gender Gap Report
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26">
        <a href="#cite_note-26">
         [26]
        </a>
       </sup>
       A large majority of Finns are members of the
       <a href="/wiki/Evangelical_Lutheran_Church_of_Finland" title="Evangelical Lutheran Church of Finland">
        Evangelical Lutheran Church
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27">
        <a href="#cite_note-27">
         [27]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and freedom of religion is guaranteed under the
       <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Finland" title="Constitution of Finland">
        Finnish Constitution
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
      </p>

        </li>
        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-47">
         <a href="#International_rankings">
          <span class="tocnumber">
           9
          </span>
          <span class="toctext">
           International rankings
          </span>
         </a>
        </li>
        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-48">

          </span>

          </span>
         </a>
        </li>

      <p>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">
        Etymology
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Finland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/Finns#Etymology" title="Finns">
        Finns § Etymology
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The earliest written appearance of the name
       <i>
        Finland
       </i>
       is thought to be on three
       <a href="/wiki/Runestone" title="Runestone">
        runestones
       </a>
       . Two were found in the Swedish province of
       <a href="/wiki/Uppland" title="Uppland">
        Uppland
       </a>
       and have the inscription
       <i>
        finlonti
       </i>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Runestone_U_582" title="Runestone U 582">
        U 582
       </a>
       ). The third was found in
       <a href="/wiki/Gotland" title="Gotland">
        Gotland
       </a>
       . It has the inscription
       <i>
        finlandi
       </i>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Runestone_G_319" title="Runestone G 319">
        G 319
       </a>
       ) and dates back to the 13th century.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28">
        <a href="#cite_note-28">
         [28]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The name can be assumed to be related to the tribe name
       <i>
        Finns
       </i>
       , which is mentioned at first known time AD 98 (disputed meaning).
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Suomi">
        Suomi
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Finland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Suomi">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The name
       <i>
        Suomi
       </i>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Finnish_language" title="Finnish language">
        Finnish
       </a>
       for
       <i>
        <span lang="fi">
         "Finland"
        </span>
       </i>
       ) has uncertain origins, but a candidate for a source is the Proto-Baltic word
       <i>
        *źemē
       </i>
       , meaning "land". In addition to the close relatives of Finnish (the
       <a href="/wiki/Finnic_languages" title="Finnic languages">
        Finnic languages
       </a>
       ), this name is also used in the
       <a href="/wiki/Baltic_languages" title="Baltic languages">
        Baltic languages
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Latvian_language" title="Latvian language">
        Latvian
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Lithuanian_language" title="Lithuanian language">
        Lithuanian
       </a>
       . Alternatively, the Indo-European word *
       <i>
        gʰm-on
       </i>
       "man" (cf. Gothic
       <i>
        guma
       </i>
       , Latin
       <i>
        homo
       </i>
       ) has been suggested, being borrowed as *
       <i>
        ćoma
       </i>
       . The word originally referred only to the province of
       <a href="/wiki/Finland_Proper_(historical_province)" title="Finland Proper (historical province)">
        Finland Proper
       </a>
       , and later to the northern coast of
       <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Finland" title="Gulf of Finland">
        Gulf of Finland
       </a>
       , with northern regions such as
       <a href="/wiki/Ostrobothnia_(historical_province)" title="Ostrobothnia (historical province)">
        Ostrobothnia
       </a>
       still sometimes being excluded until later. Earlier theories suggested derivation from
       <i>
        suomaa
       </i>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Fen" title="Fen">
        fen
       </a>
       land) or
       <i>
        suoniemi
       </i>
       (fen cape), and parallels between
       <i>
        saame
       </i>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Sami_people" title="Sami people">
        Sami
       </a>
       , a Finno-Ugric people in
       <a href="/wiki/Lapland_(Finland)" title="Lapland (Finland)">
        Lapland
       </a>
       ), and
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Tavastia_(historical_province)" title="Tavastia (historical province)">
         Häme
        </a>
       </i>
       (a province in the inland) were drawn, but these theories are now considered outdated.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29">
        <a href="#cite_note-29">
         [29]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Concept">
        Concept
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Finland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Concept">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       In the earliest historical sources from the 12th and 13th centuries, the term Finland refers to the coastal region around
       <a href="/wiki/Turku" title="Turku">
        Turku
       </a>
       from
       <a href="/wiki/Perni%C3%B6" title="Perniö">
        Perniö
       </a>
       to
       <a href="/wiki/Uusikaupunki" title="Uusikaupunki">
        Uusikaupunki
       </a>
       . This region later became known as Finland Proper in distinction from the country name Finland. Finland became a common name for the whole country in a centuries-long process that started when the
       <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">
        Catholic Church
       </a>
       established missionary diocese in
       <a href="/wiki/Nousiainen" title="Nousiainen">
        Nousiainen
       </a>
       in the northern part of the province of Suomi possibly sometime in the 12th century.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_30-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-:0-30">
         [30]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The devastation of
       <a href="/wiki/Finland_during_the_Great_Northern_War" title="Finland during the Great Northern War">
        Finland during the Great Northern War
       </a>
       (1714–1721) and during the
       <a href="/wiki/Russo-Swedish_War_(1741%E2%80%9343)" title="Russo-Swedish War (1741–43)">
        Russo-Swedish War (1741–43)
       </a>
       caused Sweden to begin carrying out major efforts to defend its eastern half from Russia. These 18th century experiences created a sense of a shared destiny that when put in conjunction with the unique Finnish language, led to the adoption of an expanded concept of Finland.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UppslagsFi_31-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-UppslagsFi-31">
         [31]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="History">
        History
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Finland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: History">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/History_of_Finland" title="History of Finland">
        History of Finland
       </a>
      </div>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Prehistory">
        Prehistory
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Finland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Prehistory">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/History_of_Finland#Prehistory" title="History of Finland">
        History of Finland § Prehistory
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kierikki_Stone_Age_Centre_Oulu_Finland_02.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1750" data-file-width="2855" height="135" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Kierikki_Stone_Age_Centre_Oulu_Finland_02.jpg/220px-Kierikki_Stone_Age_Centre_Oulu_Finland_02.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Kierikki_Stone_Age_Centre_Oulu_Finland_02.jpg/330px-Kierikki_Stone_Age_Centre_Oulu_Finland_02.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Kierikki_Stone_Age_Centre_Oulu_Finland_02.jpg/440px-Kierikki_Stone_Age_Centre_Oulu_Finland_02.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kierikki_Stone_Age_Centre_Oulu_Finland_02.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Reconstruction of
         <a href="/wiki/Stone_Age" title="Stone Age">
          Stone Age
         </a>
         dwelling from
         <a href="/wiki/Kierikki" title="Kierikki">
          Kierikki
         </a>
         , Oulu.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       If the
       <a href="/wiki/Archaeology" title="Archaeology">
        archeological
       </a>
       finds from
       <a href="/wiki/Wolf_Cave" title="Wolf Cave">
        Wolf Cave
       </a>
       are result of
       <a href="/wiki/Neanderthal" title="Neanderthal">
        Neaderthals
       </a>
       activities, the first people inhabited Finland approximately 120,000-130,000 years ago.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32">
        <a href="#cite_note-32">
         [32]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The area that is now Finland was settled in, at the latest, around 8,500 BCE during the
       <a href="/wiki/Stone_Age" title="Stone Age">
        Stone Age
       </a>
       towards the end of the
       <a href="/wiki/Last_glacial_period" title="Last glacial period">
        last glacial period
       </a>
       . The
       <a href="/wiki/Artifact_(archaeology)" title="Artifact (archaeology)">
        artifacts
       </a>
       the first settlers left behind present characteristics that are shared with those found in Estonia, Russia, and Norway.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_11-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-:1-11">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The earliest people were
       <a href="/wiki/Hunter-gatherer" title="Hunter-gatherer">
        hunter-gatherers
       </a>
       , using stone tools.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VF-Pre_12-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-VF-Pre-12">
         [12]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The first pottery appeared in 5200 BCE, when the Comb Ceramic culture was introduced.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hist-Fin-Geo_13-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Hist-Fin-Geo-13">
         [13]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The arrival of the
       <a href="/wiki/Corded_Ware_culture" title="Corded Ware culture">
        Corded Ware culture
       </a>
       in southern coastal Finland between 3000 and 2500 BCE may have coincided with the start of agriculture.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Virt-Mino_14-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Virt-Mino-14">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Even with the introduction of agriculture, hunting and fishing continued to be important parts of the subsistence economy.
      </p>
      <p>
       In the
       <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">
        Bronze Age
       </a>
       permanent all year round cultivation and
       <a href="/wiki/Animal_husbandry" title="Animal husbandry">
        animal husbandry
       </a>
       spread, but the cold climate phase slowed the change.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_33-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-:2-33">
         [33]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Cultures in Finland shared common features in pottery and also axes had similarities but local features existed.
       <a href="/wiki/Seima-Turbino_phenomenon" title="Seima-Turbino phenomenon">
        Seima-Turbino-phenomenon
       </a>
       brought first bronze artifacts to the region and possibly also the
       <a href="/wiki/Finno-Ugric_languages" title="Finno-Ugric languages">
        Finno-Ugric-Languages
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_33-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-:2-33">
         [33]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34">
        <a href="#cite_note-34">
         [34]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Commercial contacts that had so far mostly been to
       <a href="/wiki/Estonia" title="Estonia">
        Estonia
       </a>
       started to extend to Scandinavia. Domestic manufacture of bronze artifacts started 1300 BCE with
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Maaninka-type_bronze_axe&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Maaninka-type bronze axe (page does not exist)">
        Maaninka-type bronze axes
       </a>
       . Bronze was imported from
       <a href="/wiki/Volga_River" title="Volga River">
        Volga
       </a>
       region and from Southern Scandinavia.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35">
        <a href="#cite_note-35">
         [35]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Northern_Europe_in_814.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="946" data-file-width="1476" height="141" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Northern_Europe_in_814.jpg/220px-Northern_Europe_in_814.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Northern_Europe_in_814.jpg/330px-Northern_Europe_in_814.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Northern_Europe_in_814.jpg/440px-Northern_Europe_in_814.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Northern_Europe_in_814.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Northern_Europe" title="Northern Europe">
          Northern Europe
         </a>
         (814)
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       In the
       <a href="/wiki/Iron_Age" title="Iron Age">
        Iron Age
       </a>
       population grew especially in Häme and Savo regions. Finland proper was the most densely populated area. Cultural contacts to the Baltics and Scandinavia became more frequent. Commercial contacts in the
       <a href="/wiki/Baltic_Sea" title="Baltic Sea">
        Baltic Sea
       </a>
       region grew and extended during the 8th and 9th Centuries. Main exports from Finland were furs, slaves,
       <a href="/wiki/Castoreum" title="Castoreum">
        Castoreum
       </a>
       , and falcons to European courts. Imports included silk and other fabrics, jewelry,
       <a href="/wiki/Ulfberht_swords" title="Ulfberht swords">
        Ulfberht swords
       </a>
       , and, in lesser extent, glass. Production of iron started approximately in 500 BCE.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36">
        <a href="#cite_note-36">
         [36]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In the end of the 9th century indigenous artifact culture, especially women's jewelry and weapons, had more common local features than ever before. This has been interpreted to be expressing common Finnish identity which was born from an image of common origin.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37">
        <a href="#cite_note-37">
         [37]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       An early form of
       <a href="/wiki/Finnic_languages" title="Finnic languages">
        Finnic languages
       </a>
       spread to the
       <a href="/wiki/Baltic_Sea" title="Baltic Sea">
        Baltic Sea
       </a>
       region approximately 1900 BCE with the
       <a href="/wiki/Seima-Turbino_phenomenon" title="Seima-Turbino phenomenon">
        Seima-Turbino-phenomenon
       </a>
       . Common Finnic language was spoken around
       <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Finland" title="Gulf of Finland">
        Gulf of Finland
       </a>
       2000 years ago. The dialects from which the modern day Finnish language was developed came into existence during the Iron Age.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38">
        <a href="#cite_note-38">
         [38]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Although distantly related, the
       <a href="/wiki/Sami_people" title="Sami people">
        Sami
       </a>
       retained the hunter-gatherer lifestyle longer than the Finns. The Sami cultural identity and the
       <a href="/wiki/Sami_languages" title="Sami languages">
        Sami language
       </a>
       have survived in Lapland, the northernmost province, but the Sami have been displaced or assimilated elsewhere.
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Late_Iron_Age_swords_found_from_Finland.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="444" data-file-width="614" height="159" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Late_Iron_Age_swords_found_from_Finland.jpg/220px-Late_Iron_Age_swords_found_from_Finland.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Late_Iron_Age_swords_found_from_Finland.jpg/330px-Late_Iron_Age_swords_found_from_Finland.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Late_Iron_Age_swords_found_from_Finland.jpg/440px-Late_Iron_Age_swords_found_from_Finland.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Late_Iron_Age_swords_found_from_Finland.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Late
         <a href="/wiki/Iron_Age" title="Iron Age">
          Iron Age
         </a>
         swords found from Finland.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The 12th and 13th centuries were a violent time in the northern Baltic Sea. The
       <a href="/wiki/Livonian_Crusade" title="Livonian Crusade">
        Livonian crusade
       </a>
       was ongoing and the Finnish tribes such as the
       <a href="/wiki/Tavastians" title="Tavastians">
        Tavastians
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Karelia_(historical_province_of_Finland)" title="Karelia (historical province of Finland)">
        Karelians
       </a>
       were in
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Early_Finnish_Wars&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Early Finnish Wars (page does not exist)">
        frequent conflicts
       </a>
       with
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Republic_of_Novgorod" title="Republic of Novgorod">
        Novgorod
       </a>
       and with each other. Also, during the 12th and 13th centuries several crusades from the Catholic realms of the Baltic Sea area were made against the Finnish tribes. According to historical sources,
       <a href="/wiki/Danes" title="Danes">
        Danes
       </a>
       waged two crusades on Finland, in 1191 and in 1202,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39">
        <a href="#cite_note-39">
         [39]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Swedes" title="Swedes">
        Swedes
       </a>
       , possibly the so-called
       <a href="/wiki/Second_Swedish_Crusade" title="Second Swedish Crusade">
        second crusade to Finland
       </a>
       , in 1249 against
       <a href="/wiki/Tavastians" title="Tavastians">
        Tavastians
       </a>
       and the
       <a href="/wiki/Third_Swedish_Crusade" title="Third Swedish Crusade">
        third crusade to Finland
       </a>
       in 1293 against the
       <a href="/wiki/Karelia_(historical_province_of_Finland)" title="Karelia (historical province of Finland)">
        Karelians
       </a>
       . The so-called
       <a href="/wiki/First_Swedish_Crusade" title="First Swedish Crusade">
        first crusade to Finland
       </a>
       , possibly in 1155, is most likely an unreal event. Also, it is possible that Germans made violent conversion of Finnish pagans in the 13th century.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40">
        <a href="#cite_note-40">
         [40]
        </a>
       </sup>
       According to a papal letter from 1241, the king of Norway was also fighting against "nearby pagans" at that time.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41">
        <a href="#cite_note-41">
         [41]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Swedish_era">
        Swedish era
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Finland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Swedish era">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Finland_under_Swedish_rule" title="Finland under Swedish rule">
        Finland under Swedish rule
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Swedish_Empire_(1560-1815)_en2.png">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2213" data-file-width="1802" height="270" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Swedish_Empire_%281560-1815%29_en2.png/220px-Swedish_Empire_%281560-1815%29_en2.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Swedish_Empire_%281560-1815%29_en2.png/330px-Swedish_Empire_%281560-1815%29_en2.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Swedish_Empire_%281560-1815%29_en2.png/440px-Swedish_Empire_%281560-1815%29_en2.png 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Swedish_Empire_(1560-1815)_en2.png" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The
         <a href="/wiki/Swedish_Empire" title="Swedish Empire">
          Swedish Empire
         </a>
         following the
         <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Roskilde" title="Treaty of Roskilde">
          Treaty of Roskilde
         </a>
         of 1658.
         <br/>
         Dark green:
         <a href="/wiki/Sweden_proper" title="Sweden proper">
          Sweden proper
         </a>
         , as represented in the
         <a href="/wiki/Riksdag_of_the_Estates" title="Riksdag of the Estates">
          Riksdag of the Estates
         </a>
         . Other greens:
         <a href="/wiki/Dominions_of_Sweden" title="Dominions of Sweden">
          Swedish dominions
         </a>
         and
         <a href="/wiki/Possessions_of_Sweden" title="Possessions of Sweden">
          possessions
         </a>
         .
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Suomenlinna.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1360" data-file-width="1810" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Suomenlinna.jpg/220px-Suomenlinna.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Suomenlinna.jpg/330px-Suomenlinna.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Suomenlinna.jpg/440px-Suomenlinna.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Suomenlinna.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Now lying within Helsinki,
         <a href="/wiki/Suomenlinna" title="Suomenlinna">
          Suomenlinna
         </a>
         is a
         <a href="/wiki/UNESCO" title="UNESCO">
          UNESCO
         </a>
         <a href="/wiki/World_Heritage_Site" title="World Heritage Site">
          World Heritage Site
         </a>
         consisting of an inhabited 18th century sea fortress built on six islands. It is one of Finland's most popular tourist attractions.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       As a result of the crusades and the colonisation of some Finnish coastal areas with Christian
       <a href="/wiki/Swedish-speaking_population_of_Finland" title="Swedish-speaking population of Finland">
        Swedish population
       </a>
       during the Middle Ages
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42">
        <a href="#cite_note-42">
         [42]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Finland gradually became part of the kingdom of Sweden and the sphere of influence of the
       <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">
        Catholic Church
       </a>
       . Due to the Swedish conquest Finnish upper class lost its position and lands to new Swedish and German nobility and to the Catholic Church.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43">
        <a href="#cite_note-43">
         [43]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In Sweden even in the 17th and 18th centuries it was clear that Finland was conquered country and its inhabitants could be treated arbitrarily. Swedish kings visited Finland rarely and in Swedish contemporary texts Finns were portrayed to be primitive and their language inferior.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44">
        <a href="#cite_note-44">
         [44]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Finland_Swedish" title="Finland Swedish">
        Swedish
       </a>
       became the dominant language of the nobility, administration, and education;
       <a href="/wiki/Finnish_language" title="Finnish language">
        Finnish
       </a>
       was chiefly a language for the
       <a href="/wiki/Peasant" title="Peasant">
        peasantry
       </a>
       , clergy, and local
       <a href="/wiki/Court" title="Court">
        courts
       </a>
       in predominantly Finnish-speaking areas.
      </p>
      <p>
       During the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protestant_Reformation" title="Protestant Reformation">
        Protestant Reformation
       </a>
       , the
       <a href="/wiki/Finns" title="Finns">
        Finns
       </a>
       gradually converted to
       <a href="/wiki/Lutheranism" title="Lutheranism">
        Lutheranism
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45">
        <a href="#cite_note-45">
         [45]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In the 16th century,
       <a href="/wiki/Mikael_Agricola" title="Mikael Agricola">
        Mikael Agricola
       </a>
       published the first written works in Finnish. The first university in Finland, the
       <a href="/wiki/Royal_Academy_of_Turku" title="Royal Academy of Turku">
        Royal Academy of Turku
       </a>
       , was established in 1640. Finland suffered a severe
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Great_Famine_of_Finland_(1695%E2%80%931697)" title="Great Famine of Finland (1695–1697)">
        famine in 1696–1697
       </a>
       , during which about one third of the Finnish population died,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-empire_46-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-empire-46">
         [46]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_plague_during_the_Great_Northern_War" title="The plague during the Great Northern War">
        devastating plague a few years later
       </a>
       . In the 18th century, wars between Sweden and Russia twice led to the occupation of Finland by Russian forces, times known to the Finns as the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greater_Wrath" title="Greater Wrath">
        Greater Wrath
       </a>
       (1714–1721) and the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Russo-Swedish_War_(1741%E2%80%931743)" title="Russo-Swedish War (1741–1743)">
        Lesser Wrath
       </a>
       (1742–1743).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-empire_46-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-empire-46">
         [46]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It is estimated that almost an entire generation of young men was lost during the Great Wrath, due namely to the destruction of homes and farms, and to the burning of
       <a href="/wiki/Helsinki" title="Helsinki">
        Helsinki
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nordstrom,_Scandinavia_47-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Nordstrom,_Scandinavia-47">
         [47]
        </a>
       </sup>
       By this time Finland was the predominant term for the whole area from the
       <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Bothnia" title="Gulf of Bothnia">
        Gulf of Bothnia
       </a>
       to the Russian border.
      </p>
      <p>
       Two Russo-Swedish wars in twenty-five years served as reminders to the Finnish people of the precarious position between Sweden and Russia. An increasingly vocal elite in Finland soon determined that Finnish ties with Sweden were becoming too costly, and following
       <a href="/wiki/Russo-Swedish_War_(1788%E2%80%931790)" title="Russo-Swedish War (1788–1790)">
        Russo-Swedish War
       </a>
       (1788–1790), the Finnish elite's desire to break with Sweden only heightened.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-a1_48-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-a1-48">
         [48]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Even before the war there were conspiring politicians, among them
       <a href="/wiki/Georg_Magnus_Sprengtporten" title="Georg Magnus Sprengtporten">
        Col G. M. Sprengtporten
       </a>
       , who had supported
       <a href="/wiki/Gustav_III_of_Sweden#Coup_d'état" title="Gustav III of Sweden">
        Gustav III's coup in 1772
       </a>
       . Sprengporten fell out with the king and resigned his commission in 1777. In the following decade he tried to secure Russian support for an autonomous Finland, and later became an adviser to Catherine II.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-a1_48-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-a1-48">
         [48]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In the spirit of the notion of
       <a href="/wiki/Adolf_Ivar_Arwidsson" title="Adolf Ivar Arwidsson">
        Adolf Ivar Arwidsson
       </a>
       (1791–1858), "we are not Swedes, we do not want to become Russians, let us therefore be Finns", the Finnish national identity started to become established.
      </p>
      <p>
       Notwithstanding the efforts of Finland's elite and nobility to break ties with Sweden, there was no genuine independence movement in Finland until the early twentieth century. As a matter of fact, at this time the Finnish peasantry was outraged by the actions of their elite and almost exclusively supported Gustav's actions against the conspirators. (The High Court of Turku condemned Sprengtporten as a traitor c. 1793.)
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-a1_48-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-a1-48">
         [48]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Swedish era ended to
       <a href="/wiki/Finnish_War" title="Finnish War">
        Finnish war
       </a>
       in 1809.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Russian_Empire_era">
        Russian Empire era
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Finland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Russian Empire era">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Grand_Duchy_of_Finland" title="Grand Duchy of Finland">
        Grand Duchy of Finland
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/Finland%27s_language_strife" title="Finland's language strife">
        Finland's language strife
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Russification_of_Finland" title="Russification of Finland">
        Russification of Finland
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       On 29 March 1809, having been taken over by the armies of
       <a href="/wiki/Alexander_I_of_Russia" title="Alexander I of Russia">
        Alexander I of Russia
       </a>
       in the
       <a href="/wiki/Finnish_War" title="Finnish War">
        Finnish War
       </a>
       , Finland became an autonomous
       <a href="/wiki/Grand_Duchy_of_Finland" title="Grand Duchy of Finland">
        Grand Duchy
       </a>
       in the
       <a href="/wiki/Russian_Empire" title="Russian Empire">
        Russian Empire
       </a>
       until the end of 1917. In 1811, Alexander I incorporated Russian
       <a href="/wiki/Vyborg_Governorate" title="Vyborg Governorate">
        Vyborg province
       </a>
       into the Grand Duchy of Finland. During the Russian era, the Finnish language began to gain recognition. From the 1860s onwards, a strong Finnish
       <a href="/wiki/Ethnic_nationalism" title="Ethnic nationalism">
        nationalist movement
       </a>
       known as the
       <a href="/wiki/Fennoman_movement" title="Fennoman movement">
        Fennoman movement
       </a>
       grew. Milestones included the publication of what would become Finland's
       <a href="/wiki/National_epic" title="National epic">
        national epic
       </a>
       – the
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Kalevala" title="Kalevala">
         Kalevala
        </a>
       </i>
       – in 1835, and the Finnish language's achieving equal legal status with Swedish in 1892.
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pekka_Halonen_-_Tienraivaajia_Karjalassa.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1494" data-file-width="1800" height="183" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Pekka_Halonen_-_Tienraivaajia_Karjalassa.jpg/220px-Pekka_Halonen_-_Tienraivaajia_Karjalassa.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Pekka_Halonen_-_Tienraivaajia_Karjalassa.jpg/330px-Pekka_Halonen_-_Tienraivaajia_Karjalassa.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Pekka_Halonen_-_Tienraivaajia_Karjalassa.jpg/440px-Pekka_Halonen_-_Tienraivaajia_Karjalassa.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pekka_Halonen_-_Tienraivaajia_Karjalassa.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <i>
          Pioneers in Karelia
         </i>
         (1900) by
         <a href="/wiki/Pekka_Halonen" title="Pekka Halonen">
          Pekka Halonen
         </a>
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49">
          <a href="#cite_note-49">
           [49]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Finnish_famine_of_1866%E2%80%931868" title="Finnish famine of 1866–1868">
        Finnish famine of 1866–1868
       </a>
       killed 15% of the population, making it one of the worst
       <a href="/wiki/Famine" title="Famine">
        famines
       </a>
       in European history. The famine led the Russian Empire to ease financial regulations, and investment rose in following decades. Economic and political development was rapid.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-equity_50-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-equity-50">
         [50]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Gross_domestic_product" title="Gross domestic product">
        GDP
       </a>
       per capita was still half of that of the United States and a third of that of Britain.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-equity_50-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-equity-50">
         [50]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In 1906,
       <a href="/wiki/Universal_suffrage" title="Universal suffrage">
        universal suffrage
       </a>
       was adopted in the Grand Duchy of Finland. However, the relationship between the Grand Duchy and the Russian Empire soured when the Russian government made moves to restrict Finnish
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Autonomous_area" title="Autonomous area">
        autonomy
       </a>
       . For example, the universal suffrage was, in practice, virtually meaningless, since the tsar did not have to approve any of the laws adopted by the Finnish parliament. Desire for independence gained ground, first among radical liberals
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51">
        <a href="#cite_note-51">
         [51]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and socialists.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Civil_war_and_early_independence">
        Civil war and early independence
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Finland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Civil war and early independence">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main articles:
       <a href="/wiki/Finnish_Declaration_of_Independence" title="Finnish Declaration of Independence">
        Finnish Declaration of Independence
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Finnish_Civil_War" title="Finnish Civil War">
        Finnish Civil War
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:FiringsquadLankipohja.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="347" data-file-width="528" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/FiringsquadLankipohja.jpg/220px-FiringsquadLankipohja.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/FiringsquadLankipohja.jpg/330px-FiringsquadLankipohja.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/FiringsquadLankipohja.jpg/440px-FiringsquadLankipohja.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:FiringsquadLankipohja.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         White firing squad executing Red soldiers in
         <a href="/wiki/L%C3%A4ngelm%C3%A4ki" title="Längelmäki">
          Längelmäki
         </a>
         (1918)
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       After the 1917
       <a href="/wiki/February_Revolution" title="February Revolution">
        February Revolution
       </a>
       , the position of Finland as part of the Russian Empire was questioned, mainly by
       <a href="/wiki/Russian_Social_Democratic_Labour_Party" title="Russian Social Democratic Labour Party">
        Social Democrats
       </a>
       . Since the head of state was the
       <a href="/wiki/Tsar" title="Tsar">
        tsar
       </a>
       of Russia, it was not clear who the chief executive of Finland was after the revolution. The Parliament, controlled by social democrats, passed the so-called Power Act to give the highest authority to the Parliament. This was rejected by the
       <a href="/wiki/Russian_Provisional_Government" title="Russian Provisional Government">
        Russian Provisional Government
       </a>
       which decided to dissolve the Parliament.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52">
        <a href="#cite_note-52">
         [52]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       New elections were conducted, in which right-wing parties won with a slim majority. Some social democrats refused to accept the result and still claimed that the dissolution of the parliament (and thus the ensuing elections) were extralegal. The two nearly equally powerful political blocs, the right-wing parties and the social democratic party, were highly antagonized.
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/October_Revolution" title="October Revolution">
        October Revolution
       </a>
       in Russia changed the geopolitical situation anew. Suddenly, the right-wing parties in Finland started to reconsider their decision to block the transfer of highest executive power from the Russian government to Finland, as the
       <a href="/wiki/Bolsheviks" title="Bolsheviks">
        Bolsheviks
       </a>
       took power in Russia. Rather than acknowledge the authority of the
       <i>
        Power Law
       </i>
       of a few months earlier, the right-wing government
       <a href="/wiki/Finnish_Declaration_of_Independence" title="Finnish Declaration of Independence">
        declared independence
       </a>
       on 6 December 1917.
      </p>
      <p>
       On 27 January 1918, the official opening shots of the war were fired in two simultaneous events. The government started to disarm the Russian forces in
       <a href="/wiki/Ostrobothnia_(historical_province)" title="Ostrobothnia (historical province)">
        Pohjanmaa
       </a>
       , and the
       <a href="/wiki/Social_Democratic_Party_of_Finland" title="Social Democratic Party of Finland">
        Social Democratic Party
       </a>
       staged a coup.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">
          <span title="No mention of coup in the section The Finnish Civil War, which should cover this. The actions by the reds are not characterized. (December 2010)">
           not in citation given
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       The latter succeeded in controlling southern Finland and Helsinki, but the white government continued in exile from
       <a href="/wiki/Vaasa" title="Vaasa">
        Vaasa
       </a>
       . This sparked the brief but bitter
       <a href="/wiki/Finnish_Civil_War" title="Finnish Civil War">
        civil war
       </a>
       . The
       <a href="/wiki/White_Guard_(Finland)" title="White Guard (Finland)">
        Whites
       </a>
       , who were supported by
       <a href="/wiki/German_Empire" title="German Empire">
        Imperial Germany
       </a>
       , prevailed over the
       <a href="/wiki/Red_Guards_(Finland)" title="Red Guards (Finland)">
        Reds
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53">
        <a href="#cite_note-53">
         [53]
        </a>
       </sup>
       After the war, tens of thousands of Reds and suspected sympathizers were interned in camps, where thousands died by execution or from malnutrition and disease. Deep social and political enmity was sown between the Reds and Whites and would last until the
       <a href="/wiki/Winter_War" title="Winter War">
        Winter War
       </a>
       and beyond. The civil war and
       <a href="/wiki/Heimosodat" title="Heimosodat">
        activist expeditions
       </a>
       into Soviet Russia strained Eastern relations.
      </p>
      <p>
       After
       <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Finland_(1918)" title="Kingdom of Finland (1918)">
        a brief experimentation with monarchy
       </a>
       , Finland became a
       <a href="/wiki/Presidential_system" title="Presidential system">
        presidential
       </a>
       republic, with
       <a href="/wiki/Kaarlo_Juho_St%C3%A5hlberg" title="Kaarlo Juho Ståhlberg">
        Kaarlo Juho Ståhlberg
       </a>
       elected as its first president in 1919. The Finnish–Russian border was determined by the
       <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Tartu_(Russian%E2%80%93Finnish)" title="Treaty of Tartu (Russian–Finnish)">
        Treaty of Tartu
       </a>
       in 1920, largely following the historic border but granting
       <a href="/wiki/Pechengsky_District" title="Pechengsky District">
        Pechenga
       </a>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Finnish_language" title="Finnish language">
        Finnish
       </a>
       :
       <i>
        <span lang="fi">
         Petsamo
        </span>
       </i>
       ) and its
       <a href="/wiki/Barents_Sea" title="Barents Sea">
        Barents Sea
       </a>
       harbour to Finland. Finnish democracy did not see any Soviet coup attempts and survived the anti-Communist
       <a href="/wiki/Lapua_Movement" title="Lapua Movement">
        Lapua Movement
       </a>
       . The relationship between Finland and the Soviet Union was tense. Germany's relations with democratic Finland cooled also after the Nazis' rise to power.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="there was quite a lot of cooperation before the war (December 2010)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       Army officers were trained in France, and relations to Western Europe and Sweden were strengthened.
      </p>
      <p>
       In 1917, the population was 3 million. Credit-based
       <a href="/wiki/Land_reform" title="Land reform">
        land reform
       </a>
       was enacted after the civil war, increasing the proportion of capital-owning population.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-equity_50-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-equity-50">
         [50]
        </a>
       </sup>
       About 70% of workers were occupied in agriculture and 10% in industry.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54">
        <a href="#cite_note-54">
         [54]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The largest export markets were the United Kingdom and Germany.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="World_War_II_and_after">
        World War II and after
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Finland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: World War II and after">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Finnish_areas_ceded_in_1944.png">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="619" height="284" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Finnish_areas_ceded_in_1944.png/220px-Finnish_areas_ceded_in_1944.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Finnish_areas_ceded_in_1944.png/330px-Finnish_areas_ceded_in_1944.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Finnish_areas_ceded_in_1944.png/440px-Finnish_areas_ceded_in_1944.png 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Finnish_areas_ceded_in_1944.png" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Areas ceded by Finland to the
         <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">
          Soviet Union
         </a>
         after
         <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">
          World War II
         </a>
         . The
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Porkkala" title="Porkkala">
          Porkkala
         </a>
         land lease was returned to Finland in 1956
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Military_history_of_Finland_during_World_War_II" title="Military history of Finland during World War II">
        Military history of Finland during World War II
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Finland fought the
       <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">
        Soviet Union
       </a>
       twice: in the
       <a href="/wiki/Winter_War" title="Winter War">
        Winter War
       </a>
       of 1939–1940 after the Soviet Union had attacked Finland; and in the
       <a href="/wiki/Continuation_War" title="Continuation War">
        Continuation War
       </a>
       of 1941–1944, following
       <a href="/wiki/Operation_Barbarossa" title="Operation Barbarossa">
        Operation Barbarossa
       </a>
       , when Finland aligned with Germany following Germany's invasion of the Soviet Union. For 872 days, the German army, aided indirectly by Finnish forces,
       <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Leningrad" title="Siege of Leningrad">
        besieged Leningrad
       </a>
       , the USSR's second largest city.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55">
        <a href="#cite_note-55">
         [55]
        </a>
       </sup>
       After fighting a
       <a href="/wiki/Vyborg%E2%80%93Petrozavodsk_Offensive" title="Vyborg–Petrozavodsk Offensive">
        major Soviet offensive
       </a>
       in June/July 1944 to a standstill, Finland reached an armistice with the Soviet Union. This was followed by the
       <a href="/wiki/Lapland_War" title="Lapland War">
        Lapland War
       </a>
       of 1944–1945, when Finland fought against the retreating German forces in northern Finland.
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hakkapeliitta-1940.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="378" data-file-width="273" height="305" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Hakkapeliitta-1940.jpg/220px-Hakkapeliitta-1940.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c8/Hakkapeliitta-1940.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hakkapeliitta-1940.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Hakkapeliitta" title="Hakkapeliitta">
          Hakkapeliitta
         </a>
         featured on a 1940 Finnish stamp
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The treaties signed in 1947 and 1948 with the Soviet Union included Finnish obligations, restraints, and reparations—as well as further Finnish territorial concessions in addition to those in the
       <a href="/wiki/Moscow_Peace_Treaty" title="Moscow Peace Treaty">
        Moscow Peace Treaty
       </a>
       of 1940. As a result of the two wars, Finland ceded most of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Finnish_Karelia" title="Finnish Karelia">
        Finnish Karelia
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Salla" title="Salla">
        Salla
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Pechengsky_District" title="Pechengsky District">
        Petsamo
       </a>
       , which amounted to 10% of its land area and 20% of its industrial capacity, including the ports of
       <a href="/wiki/Vyborg" title="Vyborg">
        Vyborg
       </a>
       (Viipuri) and the ice-free
       <a href="/wiki/Liinakhamari" title="Liinakhamari">
        Liinakhamari
       </a>
       (Liinahamari).
       <a href="/wiki/Evacuation_of_Finnish_Karelia" title="Evacuation of Finnish Karelia">
        Almost the whole population, some 400,000 people
       </a>
       , fled these areas. Finland was never occupied by Soviet forces and it retained its independence, but at a loss of about 93,000 soldiers.
      </p>
      <p>
       Finland rejected
       <a href="/wiki/Marshall_Plan" title="Marshall Plan">
        Marshall aid
       </a>
       , in apparent
       <a href="/wiki/Finlandization" title="Finlandization">
        deference to Soviet desires
       </a>
       . However, the United States provided secret development aid and helped the Social Democratic Party, in hopes of preserving Finland's independence.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56">
        <a href="#cite_note-56">
         [56]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Establishing trade with the Western powers, such as the United Kingdom, and paying reparations to the Soviet Union produced a transformation of Finland from a primarily
       <a href="/wiki/Agriculture" title="Agriculture">
        agrarian
       </a>
       economy to an industrialised one.
       <a href="/wiki/Valmet" title="Valmet">
        Valmet
       </a>
       was founded to create materials for war reparations. After the reparations had been paid off, Finland continued to trade with the Soviet Union in the framework of
       <a href="/wiki/Bilateral_trade" title="Bilateral trade">
        bilateral trade
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Urho_Kaleva_Kekkonen.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="400" data-file-width="334" height="263" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Urho_Kaleva_Kekkonen.jpg/220px-Urho_Kaleva_Kekkonen.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Urho_Kaleva_Kekkonen.jpg/330px-Urho_Kaleva_Kekkonen.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/f5/Urho_Kaleva_Kekkonen.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Urho_Kaleva_Kekkonen.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Urho_Kekkonen" title="Urho Kekkonen">
          Urho Kekkonen
         </a>
         , the eighth president of Finland (1956–1982)
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       In 1950, 46% of Finnish workers worked in agriculture and a third lived in urban areas.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-populationdevelopment_57-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-populationdevelopment-57">
         [57]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The new jobs in manufacturing, services, and trade quickly attracted people to the towns. The average number of births per woman declined from a
       <a href="/wiki/Baby_boom" title="Baby boom">
        baby boom
       </a>
       peak of 3.5 in 1947 to 1.5 in 1973.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-populationdevelopment_57-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-populationdevelopment-57">
         [57]
        </a>
       </sup>
       When baby-boomers entered the workforce, the economy did not generate jobs fast enough, and hundreds of thousands emigrated to the more industrialized Sweden, with emigration peaking in 1969 and 1970.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-populationdevelopment_57-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-populationdevelopment-57">
         [57]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/1952_Summer_Olympics" title="1952 Summer Olympics">
        1952 Summer Olympics
       </a>
       brought international visitors. Finland took part in trade liberalization in the
       <a href="/wiki/World_Bank" title="World Bank">
        World Bank
       </a>
       , the
       <a href="/wiki/International_Monetary_Fund" title="International Monetary Fund">
        International Monetary Fund
       </a>
       and the
       <a href="/wiki/General_Agreement_on_Tariffs_and_Trade" title="General Agreement on Tariffs and Trade">
        General Agreement on Tariffs and Trade
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Officially claiming to be
       <a href="/wiki/Neutral_country" title="Neutral country">
        neutral
       </a>
       , Finland lay in the grey zone between the
       <a href="/wiki/Western_world" title="Western world">
        Western countries
       </a>
       and the Soviet Union. The
       <a href="/wiki/Finno-Soviet_Treaty_of_1948" title="Finno-Soviet Treaty of 1948">
        YYA Treaty
       </a>
       (Finno-Soviet Pact of Friendship, Cooperation and Mutual Assistance) gave the Soviet Union some leverage in Finnish domestic politics. This was extensively exploited by president
       <a href="/wiki/Urho_Kekkonen" title="Urho Kekkonen">
        Urho Kekkonen
       </a>
       against his opponents. He maintained an effective monopoly on Soviet relations from 1956 on, which was crucial for his continued popularity. In politics, there was a tendency of avoiding any policies and statements that could be interpreted as anti-Soviet. This phenomenon was given the name "
       <a href="/wiki/Finlandization" title="Finlandization">
        Finlandization
       </a>
       " by the West German press.
      </p>
      <p>
       Despite close relations with the Soviet Union, Finland maintained a market economy. Various industries benefited from
       <a href="/wiki/Bilateral_trade" title="Bilateral trade">
        trade privileges
       </a>
       with the Soviets, which explains the widespread support that pro-Soviet policies enjoyed among business interests in Finland. Economic growth was rapid in the postwar era, and by 1975 Finland's GDP per capita was the 15th highest in the world. In the 1970s and 80s, Finland built one of the most extensive
       <a href="/wiki/Welfare_state" title="Welfare state">
        welfare states
       </a>
       in the world. Finland negotiated with the
       <a href="/wiki/European_Economic_Community" title="European Economic Community">
        EEC
       </a>
       (a predecessor of the European Union) a treaty that mostly abolished customs duties towards the EEC starting from 1977, although Finland did not fully join. In 1981, president
       <a href="/wiki/Urho_Kekkonen" title="Urho Kekkonen">
        Urho Kekkonen
       </a>
       's failing health forced him to retire after holding office for 25 years.
      </p>
      <p>
       Finland reacted cautiously to the collapse of the Soviet Union, but swiftly began increasing integration with the West. On 21 September 1990, Finland unilaterally declared the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paris_Peace_Treaty" title="Paris Peace Treaty">
        Paris Peace Treaty
       </a>
       obsolete, following the German reunification decision nine days earlier.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58">
        <a href="#cite_note-58">
         [58]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tratado_de_Lisboa_13_12_2007_(081).jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="310" data-file-width="521" height="131" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Tratado_de_Lisboa_13_12_2007_%28081%29.jpg/220px-Tratado_de_Lisboa_13_12_2007_%28081%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Tratado_de_Lisboa_13_12_2007_%28081%29.jpg/330px-Tratado_de_Lisboa_13_12_2007_%28081%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Tratado_de_Lisboa_13_12_2007_%28081%29.jpg/440px-Tratado_de_Lisboa_13_12_2007_%28081%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tratado_de_Lisboa_13_12_2007_(081).jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Finland joined the European Union in 1995 and signed the
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lisbon_Treaty" title="Lisbon Treaty">
          Lisbon Treaty
         </a>
         in 2007
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
