      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ecker%C3%B6_Line_-_Finlandia.JPG">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2736" data-file-width="3648" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Ecker%C3%B6_Line_-_Finlandia.JPG/220px-Ecker%C3%B6_Line_-_Finlandia.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Ecker%C3%B6_Line_-_Finlandia.JPG/330px-Ecker%C3%B6_Line_-_Finlandia.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Ecker%C3%B6_Line_-_Finlandia.JPG/440px-Ecker%C3%B6_Line_-_Finlandia.JPG 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ecker%C3%B6_Line_-_Finlandia.JPG" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Ecker%C3%B6_Line" title="Eckerö Line">
          Eckerö Line
         </a>
         Finlandia, one of the
         <a href="/wiki/Baltic_Sea" title="Baltic Sea">
          Baltic Sea
         </a>
         cruiseferries
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       In 2005, Finnish tourism grossed over €6.7 billion with a 5% increase from the previous year. Much of the sudden growth can be attributed to the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Globalisation" title="Globalisation">
        globalisation
       </a>
       and modernisation of the country as well as a rise in positive publicity and awareness. There are many attractions in Finland which attracted over 8 million visitors in 2013.
      </p>
      <p>
       The Finnish landscape is covered with thick
       <a href="/wiki/Pine" title="Pine">
        pine
       </a>
       forests and rolling hills, and complemented with a labyrinth of lakes and
       <a href="/wiki/Inlet" title="Inlet">
        inlets
       </a>
       . Much of Finland is pristine and virgin as it contains 40 national parks from the Southern shores of the
       <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Finland" title="Gulf of Finland">
        Gulf of Finland
       </a>
       to the high
       <a href="/wiki/Fell" title="Fell">
        fells
       </a>
       of Lapland. Finland also has urbanised regions with many cultural events and activities.
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Baltic_Sea_cruiseferries" title="Baltic Sea cruiseferries">
        Commercial cruises
       </a>
       between major coastal and port cities in the Baltic region, including Helsinki,
       <a href="/wiki/Turku" title="Turku">
        Turku
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Tallinn" title="Tallinn">
        Tallinn
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Stockholm" title="Stockholm">
        Stockholm
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Travem%C3%BCnde" title="Travemünde">
        Travemünde
       </a>
       , play a significant role in the local tourism industry. Finland is locally regarded as the home of Saint Nicholas or
       <a href="/wiki/Santa_Claus" title="Santa Claus">
        Santa Claus
       </a>
       , living in the northern Lapland region.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143">
        <a href="#cite_note-143">
         [142]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Above the
       <a href="/wiki/Arctic_Circle" title="Arctic Circle">
        Arctic Circle
       </a>
       , in midwinter, there is a
       <a href="/wiki/Polar_night" title="Polar night">
        polar night
       </a>
       , a period when the sun does not rise for days or weeks, or even months, and correspondingly,
       <a href="/wiki/Midnight_sun" title="Midnight sun">
        midnight sun
       </a>
       in the summer, with no sunset even at midnight (for up to 73 consecutive days, at the northernmost point). Lapland is so far north that the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aurora_Borealis" title="Aurora Borealis">
        Aurora Borealis
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Fluorescence" title="Fluorescence">
        fluorescence
       </a>
       in the high atmosphere due to
       <a href="/wiki/Solar_wind" title="Solar wind">
        solar wind
       </a>
       , is seen regularly in the fall, winter, and spring.
      </p>
      <p>
       Outdoor activities range from
       <a href="/wiki/Nordic_skiing" title="Nordic skiing">
        Nordic skiing
       </a>
       , golf, fishing,
       <a href="/wiki/Yachting" title="Yachting">
        yachting
       </a>
       , lake cruises, hiking, and
       <a href="/wiki/Kayaking" title="Kayaking">
        kayaking
       </a>
       , among many others. Wildlife is abundant in Finland.
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bird-watching" title="Bird-watching">
        Bird-watching
       </a>
       is popular for those fond of avifauna, however hunting is also popular.
       <a href="/wiki/Moose" title="Moose">
        Elk
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Hare" title="Hare">
        hare
       </a>
       are common game in Finland.
       <a href="/wiki/Olavinlinna" title="Olavinlinna">
        Olavinlinna
       </a>
       in
       <a href="/wiki/Savonlinna" title="Savonlinna">
        Savonlinna
       </a>
       hosts the annual
       <a href="/wiki/Savonlinna_Opera_Festival" title="Savonlinna Opera Festival">
        Savonlinna Opera Festival
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Demographics">
        Demographics
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Finland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Demographics">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Demographics_of_Finland" title="Demographics of Finland">
        Demographics of Finland
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Population_pyramid_finland_2013.png">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="926" data-file-width="779" height="202" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Population_pyramid_finland_2013.png/170px-Population_pyramid_finland_2013.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Population_pyramid_finland_2013.png/255px-Population_pyramid_finland_2013.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Population_pyramid_finland_2013.png/340px-Population_pyramid_finland_2013.png 2x" width="170"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Population_pyramid_finland_2013.png" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Population of Finland by age group and sex in 2013
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The population of Finland is currently about 5.5 million inhabitants and is aging with the
       <a href="/wiki/Birth_rate" title="Birth rate">
        birth rate
       </a>
       at 10.42 births per 1,000 population per year, or a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fertility_rate" title="Fertility rate">
        fertility rate
       </a>
       of 1.8 with a median age of 42.7 years. Approximately half of voters are estimated to be over 50 years old.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144">
        <a href="#cite_note-144">
         [143]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-populationdevelopment_57-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-populationdevelopment-57">
         [57]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145">
        <a href="#cite_note-145">
         [144]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pop_stat_8-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-pop_stat-8">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Finland has an average population density of 18 inhabitants per square kilometre. This is the third-lowest population density of any European country, behind those of Norway and
       <a href="/wiki/Iceland" title="Iceland">
        Iceland
       </a>
       , and the lowest population density in the EU. Finland's population has always been concentrated in the southern parts of the country, a phenomenon that became even more pronounced during 20th-century urbanisation. The largest cities in Finland are those of the
       <a href="/wiki/Greater_Helsinki" title="Greater Helsinki">
        Greater Helsinki
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_area" title="Metropolitan area">
        metropolitan area
       </a>
       —
       <a href="/wiki/Helsinki" title="Helsinki">
        Helsinki
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Espoo" title="Espoo">
        Espoo
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Vantaa" title="Vantaa">
        Vantaa
       </a>
       . Other cities with population over 100,000 are
       <a href="/wiki/Tampere" title="Tampere">
        Tampere
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Turku" title="Turku">
        Turku
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Oulu" title="Oulu">
        Oulu
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Jyv%C3%A4skyl%C3%A4" title="Jyväskylä">
        Jyväskylä
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Kuopio" title="Kuopio">
        Kuopio
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Lahti" title="Lahti">
        Lahti
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       As of 2014, there were 322,700 people with a foreign background living in Finland (5.9% of the population), most of whom are from Russia, Estonia, Somalia, Iraq and Yugoslavia.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146">
        <a href="#cite_note-146">
         [145]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The children of foreigners are not automatically given Finnish citizenship, as Finnish nationality law practices and maintain
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Jus_sanguinis" title="Jus sanguinis">
         jus sanguinis
        </a>
       </i>
       policy where only children born to at least one Finnish parent are granted citizenship. If they are born in Finland and cannot get citizenship of any other country, they become citizens.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147">
        <a href="#cite_note-147">
         [146]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Additionally, certain persons of Finnish descent who reside in countries that were once part of
       <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">
        Soviet Union
       </a>
       , retain the
       <a href="/wiki/Right_of_return" title="Right of return">
        right of return
       </a>
       , a right to establish permanent residency in the country, which would eventually entitle them to qualify for citizenship.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148">
        <a href="#cite_note-148">
         [147]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>

      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">

       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">

       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Catedral_Luterana_de_Helsinki,_Finlandia,_2012-08-14,_DD_01.JPG">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3448" data-file-width="4012" height="189" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Catedral_Luterana_de_Helsinki%2C_Finlandia%2C_2012-08-14%2C_DD_01.JPG/220px-Catedral_Luterana_de_Helsinki%2C_Finlandia%2C_2012-08-14%2C_DD_01.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Catedral_Luterana_de_Helsinki%2C_Finlandia%2C_2012-08-14%2C_DD_01.JPG/330px-Catedral_Luterana_de_Helsinki%2C_Finlandia%2C_2012-08-14%2C_DD_01.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Catedral_Luterana_de_Helsinki%2C_Finlandia%2C_2012-08-14%2C_DD_01.JPG/440px-Catedral_Luterana_de_Helsinki%2C_Finlandia%2C_2012-08-14%2C_DD_01.JPG 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Catedral_Luterana_de_Helsinki,_Finlandia,_2012-08-14,_DD_01.JPG" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The Evangelical Lutheran
         <a href="/wiki/Helsinki_Cathedral" title="Helsinki Cathedral">
          Helsinki Cathedral
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       With slightly under four million members, the Evangelical Lutheran Church of Finland is one of the largest Lutheran churches in the world and is also by far Finland's largest religious body; at the end of 2016, 71.9% of Finns were members of the church.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-evl_stat_2016_150-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-evl_stat_2016-150">
         [149]
        </a>
       </sup>
       7 out of 10 Finns are members of the
       <a href="/wiki/Evangelical_Lutheran_Church_of_Finland" title="Evangelical Lutheran Church of Finland">
        Evangelical Lutheran Church of Finland
       </a>
       , which was
       <a href="/wiki/Separation_of_church_and_state" title="Separation of church and state">
        disestablished
       </a>
       in 1869 by the Church Act. It was the first state church to be disestablished in the
       <a href="/wiki/Nordic_countries" title="Nordic countries">
        Nordic countries
       </a>
       , to be followed by the
       <a href="/wiki/Church_of_Sweden" title="Church of Sweden">
        Church of Sweden
       </a>
       in 2000. The Evangelical Lutheran Church of Finland sees its share of the country's population declining by roughly one percent annually in recent years.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-relig_stat_149-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-relig_stat-149">
         [148]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The decline has been due to both church membership resignations and falling baptism rates.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151">
        <a href="#cite_note-151">
         [150]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152">
        <a href="#cite_note-152">
         [151]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The second largest group, accounting for 25.3% of the population
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-relig_stat_149-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-relig_stat-149">
         [148]
        </a>
       </sup>
       in 2016, has no religious affiliation. The irreligious group rose quickly from just below 13% in the year 2000. A small minority belongs to the
       <a href="/wiki/Finnish_Orthodox_Church" title="Finnish Orthodox Church">
        Finnish Orthodox Church
       </a>
       (1.1%). Other
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protestant" title="Protestant">
        Protestant
       </a>
       denominations and the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_Church" title="Roman Catholic Church">
        Roman Catholic Church
       </a>
       are significantly smaller, as are the
       <a href="/wiki/Muslim" title="Muslim">
        Muslim
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jewish" title="Jewish">
        Jewish
       </a>
       , and other non-Christian communities (totalling 1.6%). The main Lutheran and Orthodox churches are
       <a href="/wiki/National_church" title="National church">
        national churches
       </a>
       of Finland with special roles such as in state ceremonies and schools.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153">
        <a href="#cite_note-153">
         [152]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In 1869, Finland was the first Nordic country to
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Disestablishment" title="Disestablishment">
        disestablish
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Evangelical_Lutheran_Church_of_Finland" title="Evangelical Lutheran Church of Finland">
        its Evangelical Lutheran church
       </a>
       by introducing the Church Act. Although the church still maintains a special relationship with the state, it is not described as a
       <a href="/wiki/State_religion" title="State religion">
        state religion
       </a>
       in the
       <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Finland" title="Constitution of Finland">
        Finnish Constitution
       </a>
       or other laws passed by the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Finnish_Parliament" title="Finnish Parliament">
        Finnish Parliament
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FinlandConstitution_154-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FinlandConstitution-154">
         [153]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Finland's state church was the
       <a href="/wiki/Church_of_Sweden" title="Church of Sweden">
        Church of Sweden
       </a>
       until 1809. As an autonomous Grand Duchy under Russia 1809–1917, Finland retained the Lutheran State Church system, and a state church separate from Sweden, later named the
       <a href="/wiki/Evangelical_Lutheran_Church_of_Finland" title="Evangelical Lutheran Church of Finland">
        Evangelical Lutheran Church of Finland
       </a>
       , was established. It was detached from the state as a separate judicial entity when the new church law came to force in 1869. After Finland had gained independence in 1917, religious freedom was declared in the constitution of 1919 and a separate law on religious freedom in 1922. Through this arrangement, the Evangelical Lutheran Church of Finland lost its position as a state church but gained a constitutional status as a national church alongside the
       <a href="/wiki/Finnish_Orthodox_Church" title="Finnish Orthodox Church">
        Finnish Orthodox Church
       </a>
       , whose position however is not codified in the constitution.
      </p>
      <p>
       In 2016, 69.3% of Finnish children were
       <a href="/wiki/Baptism" title="Baptism">
        baptized
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155">
        <a href="#cite_note-155">
         [154]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and 82.3% were
       <a href="/wiki/Confirmation_(Lutheran_Church)" title="Confirmation (Lutheran Church)">
        confirmed
       </a>
       in 2012 at the age of 15,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156">
        <a href="#cite_note-156">
         [155]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and over 90% of the funerals are Christian. However, the majority of Lutherans attend church only for special occasions like Christmas ceremonies, weddings, and funerals. The Lutheran Church estimates that approximately 1.8% of its members attend church services weekly.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157">
        <a href="#cite_note-157">
         [156]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The average number of church visits per year by church members is approximately two.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-state2004_158-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-state2004-158">
         [157]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       According to a 2010
       <a href="/wiki/Eurobarometer" title="Eurobarometer">
        Eurobarometer
       </a>
       poll, 33% of Finnish citizens responded that "they believe there is a God"; 42% answered that "they believe there is some sort of spirit or life force"; and 22% that "they do not believe there is any sort of spirit, God, or life force".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EUROBAROMETER_159-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-EUROBAROMETER-159">
         [158]
        </a>
       </sup>
       According to ISSP survey data (2008), 8% consider themselves "highly religious", and 31% "moderately religious". In the same survey, 28% reported themselves as "agnostic" and 29% as "non-religious".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160">
        <a href="#cite_note-160">
         [159]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Health">
        Health
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Finland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Health">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Healthcare_in_Finland" title="Healthcare in Finland">
        Healthcare in Finland
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Life_expectancy" title="Life expectancy">
        Life expectancy
       </a>
       has increased from 71 years for men and 79 years for women in 1990 to 78 years for men and 84 years for women in 2012.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161">
        <a href="#cite_note-161">
         [160]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The under-five mortality rate has decreased from 51 per 1,000 live births in 1950 to 3 per 1,000 live births in 2012 ranking Finland's rate among the lowest in the world.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162">
        <a href="#cite_note-162">
         [161]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163">
        <a href="#cite_note-163">
         [162]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fertility_rate" title="Fertility rate">
        fertility rate
       </a>
       in 2014 stood at 1.71 children born/per woman and has been below
       <a href="/wiki/Sub-replacement_fertility" title="Sub-replacement fertility">
        the replacement rate
       </a>
       of 2.1 since 1969.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stat.fi_164-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-stat.fi-164">
         [163]
        </a>
       </sup>
       With a low birth rate women also become mothers at a later age, the mean age at first live birth being 28.6 in 2014.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stat.fi_164-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-stat.fi-164">
         [163]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       There has been a slight increase or no change in welfare and health inequalities between population groups in the 21st century. Lifestyle-related diseases are on the rise. More than half a million Finns suffer from diabetes, type 1 diabetes being globally the most common in Finland. Many children are diagnosed with type 2 diabetes. The number of musculoskeletal diseases and cancers are increasing, although the cancer prognosis has improved. Allergies and dementia are also growing health problems in Finland. One of the most common reasons for work disability are due to mental disorders, in particular depression.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165">
        <a href="#cite_note-165">
         [164]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       There are 307 residents for each doctor.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166">
        <a href="#cite_note-166">
         [165]
        </a>
       </sup>
       About 19% of health care is funded directly by households and 77% by taxation.
      </p>
      <p>
       A recent study by The Lancet medical journal found that Finland has the lowest stillbirth rate out of 193 countries, including the UK, France, and New Zealand.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167">
        <a href="#cite_note-167">
         [166]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In April 2012, Finland was ranked 2nd in Gross National Happiness in a report published by The Earth Institute.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168">
        <a href="#cite_note-168">
         [167]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Education_and_science">
        Education and science
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Finland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Education and science">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Helsinki_University_of_Technology_auditorium.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="450" data-file-width="666" height="149" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Helsinki_University_of_Technology_auditorium.jpg/220px-Helsinki_University_of_Technology_auditorium.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Helsinki_University_of_Technology_auditorium.jpg/330px-Helsinki_University_of_Technology_auditorium.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Helsinki_University_of_Technology_auditorium.jpg/440px-Helsinki_University_of_Technology_auditorium.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Helsinki_University_of_Technology_auditorium.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Auditorium in
         <a href="/wiki/Aalto_University" title="Aalto University">
          Aalto University
         </a>
         's main building, designed by
         <a href="/wiki/Alvar_Aalto" title="Alvar Aalto">
          Alvar Aalto
         </a>
         .
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Torvisen_kansakoulu_1924-26.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="361" data-file-width="554" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Torvisen_kansakoulu_1924-26.jpg/220px-Torvisen_kansakoulu_1924-26.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Torvisen_kansakoulu_1924-26.jpg/330px-Torvisen_kansakoulu_1924-26.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Torvisen_kansakoulu_1924-26.jpg/440px-Torvisen_kansakoulu_1924-26.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Torvisen_kansakoulu_1924-26.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Pupils at the school of Torvinen in Sodankylä, Finland, in the 1920s
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Education_in_Finland" title="Education in Finland">
        Education in Finland
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/List_of_universities_in_Finland" title="List of universities in Finland">
        List of universities in Finland
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/List_of_schools_in_Finland" title="List of schools in Finland">
        List of schools in Finland
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Most pre-tertiary education is arranged at municipal level. Even though many or most schools were started as private schools, today only around 3 percent of students are enrolled in private schools (mostly specialist language and international schools), much less than in Sweden and most other developed countries.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169">
        <a href="#cite_note-169">
         [168]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Pre-school education is rare compared with other EU countries and formal education is usually started at the age of 7. Primary school takes normally six years and lower secondary school three years. Most schools are managed by municipal officials.
      </p>
      <p>
       The flexible curriculum is set by the Ministry of Education and the Education Board. Education is compulsory between the ages of 7 and 16. After lower secondary school, graduates may either enter the workforce directly, or apply to trade schools or
       <a href="/wiki/Gymnasium_(school)" title="Gymnasium (school)">
        gymnasiums
       </a>
       (upper secondary schools). Trade schools offer a
       <a href="/wiki/Vocational_education" title="Vocational education">
        vocational education
       </a>
       : approximately 40% of an age group choose this path after the lower secondary school.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170">
        <a href="#cite_note-170">
         [169]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Academically oriented gymnasiums have higher entrance requirements and specifically prepare for
       <a href="/wiki/Abitur" title="Abitur">
        Abitur
       </a>
       and tertiary education. Graduation from either formally qualifies for tertiary education.
      </p>
      <p>
       In tertiary education, two mostly separate and non-interoperating sectors are found: the profession-oriented polytechnics and the research-oriented universities. Education is free and living expenses are to a large extent financed by the government through
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Student_benefit" title="Student benefit">
        student benefits
       </a>
       . There are 20 universities and 30 polytechnics in the country. The
       <a href="/wiki/University_of_Helsinki" title="University of Helsinki">
        University of Helsinki
       </a>
       is ranked 75th in the Top University Ranking of 2010.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171">
        <a href="#cite_note-171">
         [170]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/World_Economic_Forum" title="World Economic Forum">
        World Economic Forum
       </a>
       ranks Finland's tertiary education No. 1 in the world.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172">
        <a href="#cite_note-172">
         [171]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Around 33% of residents have a tertiary degree, similar to Nordics and more than in most other OECD countries except Canada (44%), United States (38%) and Japan (37%).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173">
        <a href="#cite_note-173">
         [172]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The proportion of foreign students is 3% of all tertiary enrollments, one of the lowest in OECD, while in advanced programs it is 7.3%, still below OECD average 16.5%.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174">
        <a href="#cite_note-174">
         [173]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       More than 30% of tertiary graduates are in science-related fields. Forest improvement, materials research, environmental sciences, neural networks, low-temperature physics, brain research, biotechnology, genetic technology, and communications showcase fields of study where Finnish researchers have had a significant impact.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-innovation_175-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-innovation-175">
         [174]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Finland has a long tradition of adult education, and by the 1980s nearly one million Finns were receiving some kind of instruction each year. Forty percent of them did so for professional reasons. Adult education appeared in a number of forms, such as secondary evening schools, civic and workers' institutes, study centres, vocational course centres, and folk high schools. Study centres allowed groups to follow study plans of their own making, with educational and financial assistance provided by the state.
       <a href="/wiki/Folk_high_school" title="Folk high school">
        Folk high schools
       </a>
       are a distinctly Nordic institution. Originating in Denmark in the nineteenth century, folk high schools became common throughout the region. Adults of all ages could stay at them for several weeks and take courses in subjects that ranged from handicrafts to economics.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LOC_95-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-LOC-95">
         [94]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Finland is highly productive in scientific research. In 2005, Finland had the fourth most scientific publications per capita of the OECD countries.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176">
        <a href="#cite_note-176">
         [175]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In 2007, 1,801 patents were filed in Finland.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177">
        <a href="#cite_note-177">
         [176]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In addition, 38 percent of Finland's population has a university or
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/College_degree" title="College degree">
        college degree
       </a>
       , which is among the highest percentages in the
       <a href="/wiki/World" title="World">
        world
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178">
        <a href="#cite_note-178">
         [177]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179">
        <a href="#cite_note-179">
         [178]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In 2010 a new law was enacted considering the universities, which defined that there are 16 of them as they were excluded from the public sector to be autonomous legal and financial entities, however enjoying special status in the legislation.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180">
        <a href="#cite_note-180">
         [179]
        </a>
       </sup>
       As result many former state institutions were driven to collect funding from private sector contributions and partnerships. The change caused deep rooted discussions among the academic circles.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181">
        <a href="#cite_note-181">
         [180]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       English language is important in Finnish education. There are a number of degree programs that are taught in English, which attracts thousands of degree and exchange students every year.
      </p>
      <p>
       In December 2017 the
       <a href="/wiki/OECD" title="OECD">
        OECD
       </a>
       reported that Finnish fathers spend an average of eight minutes a day more with their school-aged children than mothers do.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182">
        <a href="#cite_note-182">
         [181]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183">
        <a href="#cite_note-183">
         [182]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Culture">
        Culture
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Finland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Culture">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mikael_Agricola_by_Albert_Edelfelt.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="716" data-file-width="546" height="223" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Mikael_Agricola_by_Albert_Edelfelt.jpg/170px-Mikael_Agricola_by_Albert_Edelfelt.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Mikael_Agricola_by_Albert_Edelfelt.jpg/255px-Mikael_Agricola_by_Albert_Edelfelt.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Mikael_Agricola_by_Albert_Edelfelt.jpg/340px-Mikael_Agricola_by_Albert_Edelfelt.jpg 2x" width="170"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mikael_Agricola_by_Albert_Edelfelt.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Mikael_Agricola" title="Mikael Agricola">
          Mikael Agricola
         </a>
         , a Lutheran Protestant reformer and the father of the Finnish written language.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_Finland" title="Culture of Finland">
        Culture of Finland
       </a>
      </div>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Literature">
        Literature
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Finland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Literature">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Finnish_literature" title="Finnish literature">
        Finnish literature
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Written Finnish could be said to have existed since
       <a href="/wiki/Mikael_Agricola" title="Mikael Agricola">
        Mikael Agricola
       </a>
       translated the
       <a href="/wiki/New_Testament" title="New Testament">
        New Testament
       </a>
       into Finnish during the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protestant_Reformation" title="Protestant Reformation">
        Protestant Reformation
       </a>
       , but few notable works of literature were written until the nineteenth century and the beginning of a Finnish national
       <a href="/wiki/Romanticism" title="Romanticism">
        Romantic Movement
       </a>
       . This prompted
       <a href="/wiki/Elias_L%C3%B6nnrot" title="Elias Lönnrot">
        Elias Lönnrot
       </a>
       to collect Finnish and Karelian folk poetry and arrange and publish them as the
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Kalevala" title="Kalevala">
         Kalevala
        </a>
       </i>
       , the Finnish
       <a href="/wiki/National_epic" title="National epic">
        national epic
       </a>
       . The era saw a rise of poets and novelists who wrote in Finnish, notably
       <a href="/wiki/Aleksis_Kivi" title="Aleksis Kivi">
        Aleksis Kivi
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Eino_Leino" title="Eino Leino">
        Eino Leino
       </a>
       . Many writers of the national awakening wrote in Swedish, such as the national poet
       <a href="/wiki/Johan_Ludvig_Runeberg" title="Johan Ludvig Runeberg">
        Johan Ludvig Runeberg
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Zachris_Topelius" title="Zachris Topelius">
        Zachris Topelius
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       After Finland became independent, there was a rise of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Modernist_literature" title="Modernist literature">
        modernist writers
       </a>
       , most famously the Finnish-speaking
       <a href="/wiki/Mika_Waltari" title="Mika Waltari">
        Mika Waltari
       </a>
       and Swedish-speaking
       <a href="/wiki/Edith_S%C3%B6dergran" title="Edith Södergran">
        Edith Södergran
       </a>
       .
       <a href="/wiki/Frans_Eemil_Sillanp%C3%A4%C3%A4" title="Frans Eemil Sillanpää">
        Frans Eemil Sillanpää
       </a>
       was awarded the
       <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Literature" title="Nobel Prize in Literature">
        Nobel Prize in Literature
       </a>
       in 1939. World War II prompted a return to more national interests in comparison to a more international line of thought, characterized by
       <a href="/wiki/V%C3%A4in%C3%B6_Linna" title="Väinö Linna">
        Väinö Linna
       </a>
       . Besides Kalevala and Waltari, the Swedish-speaking
       <a href="/wiki/Tove_Jansson" title="Tove Jansson">
        Tove Jansson
       </a>
       is the most translated Finnish writer. Popular modern writers include
       <a href="/wiki/Arto_Paasilinna" title="Arto Paasilinna">
        Arto Paasilinna
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Ilkka_Remes" title="Ilkka Remes">
        Ilkka Remes
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Kari_Hotakainen" title="Kari Hotakainen">
        Kari Hotakainen
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Sofi_Oksanen" title="Sofi Oksanen">
        Sofi Oksanen
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Jari_Tervo" title="Jari Tervo">
        Jari Tervo
       </a>
       , while the best novel is annually awarded the prestigious
       <a href="/wiki/Finlandia_Prize" title="Finlandia Prize">
        Finlandia Prize
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sammon_puolustus.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1758" data-file-width="1788" height="216" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Sammon_puolustus.jpg/220px-Sammon_puolustus.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Sammon_puolustus.jpg/330px-Sammon_puolustus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Sammon_puolustus.jpg/440px-Sammon_puolustus.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sammon_puolustus.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Akseli_Gallen-Kallela" title="Akseli Gallen-Kallela">
          Akseli Gallen-Kallela
         </a>
         ,
         <i>
          <a href="/wiki/The_Defense_of_the_Sampo" title="The Defense of the Sampo">
           The Defense of the Sampo
          </a>
         </i>
         , 1896,
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Turku_Art_Museum" title="Turku Art Museum">
          Turku Art Museum
         </a>
         .
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <h3>
       <span id="Visual_arts.2C_design.2C_and_architecture">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Visual_arts,_design,_and_architecture">
        Visual arts, design, and architecture
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Finland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Visual arts, design, and architecture">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/Architecture_of_Finland" title="Architecture of Finland">
        Architecture of Finland
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Art_in_Finland" title="Art in Finland">
        Art in Finland
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The visual arts in Finland started to form their individual characteristics in the 19th century, when Romantic nationalism was rising in autonomic Finland. The best known of Finnish painters,
       <a href="/wiki/Akseli_Gallen-Kallela" title="Akseli Gallen-Kallela">
        Akseli Gallen-Kallela
       </a>
       , started painting in a naturalist style, but moved to national romanticism. Finland's best-known sculptor of the twentieth century was
       <a href="/wiki/W%C3%A4in%C3%B6_Aaltonen" title="Wäinö Aaltonen">
        Wäinö Aaltonen
       </a>
       , remembered for his monumental
       <a href="/wiki/Bust_(sculpture)" title="Bust (sculpture)">
        busts
       </a>
       and sculptures. Finns have made major contributions to
       <a href="/wiki/Handicraft" title="Handicraft">
        handicrafts
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Industrial_design" title="Industrial design">
        industrial design
       </a>
       : among the internationally renowned figures are
       <a href="/wiki/Timo_Sarpaneva" title="Timo Sarpaneva">
        Timo Sarpaneva
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Tapio_Wirkkala" title="Tapio Wirkkala">
        Tapio Wirkkala
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Ilmari_Tapiovaara" title="Ilmari Tapiovaara">
        Ilmari Tapiovaara
       </a>
       . Finnish architecture is famous around the world, and has contributed significantly to several styles internationally, such as
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jugendstil" title="Jugendstil">
        Jugendstil
       </a>
       (or
       <a href="/wiki/Art_Nouveau" title="Art Nouveau">
        Art Nouveau
       </a>
       ),
       <a href="/wiki/Nordic_Classicism" title="Nordic Classicism">
        Nordic Classicism
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Functionalism_(architecture)" title="Functionalism (architecture)">
        Functionalism
       </a>
       . Among the top twentieth-century Finnish architects to gain international recognition are
       <a href="/wiki/Eliel_Saarinen" title="Eliel Saarinen">
        Eliel Saarinen
       </a>
       and his son
       <a href="/wiki/Eero_Saarinen" title="Eero Saarinen">
        Eero Saarinen
       </a>
       . Architect
       <a href="/wiki/Alvar_Aalto" title="Alvar Aalto">
        Alvar Aalto
       </a>
       is regarded as among the most important twentieth-century designers in the world;
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184">
        <a href="#cite_note-184">
         [183]
        </a>
       </sup>
       he helped bring
       <a href="/wiki/Functionalism_(architecture)" title="Functionalism (architecture)">
        functionalist architecture
       </a>
       to Finland, but soon was a pioneer in its development towards an organic style.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185">
        <a href="#cite_note-185">
         [184]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Aalto is also famous for his work in furniture, lamps, textiles and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glassware" title="Glassware">
        glassware
       </a>
       , which were usually incorporated into his buildings.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Music">
        Music
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Finland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Music">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jean_Sibelius,_1913.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2496" data-file-width="1872" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Jean_Sibelius%2C_1913.jpg/170px-Jean_Sibelius%2C_1913.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Jean_Sibelius%2C_1913.jpg/255px-Jean_Sibelius%2C_1913.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Jean_Sibelius%2C_1913.jpg/340px-Jean_Sibelius%2C_1913.jpg 2x" width="170"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jean_Sibelius,_1913.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The Finnish composer
         <a href="/wiki/Jean_Sibelius" title="Jean Sibelius">
          Jean Sibelius
         </a>
         (1865–1957), a significant figure in the history of
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/European_classical_music" title="European classical music">
          classical music
         </a>
         .
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main articles:
       <a href="/wiki/Music_of_Finland" title="Music of Finland">
        Music of Finland
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Rock_music_in_Finland" title="Rock music in Finland">
        Rock music in Finland
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Sami_music" title="Sami music">
        Sami music
       </a>
      </div>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Classical">
        Classical
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Finland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Classical">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       Much of Finland's classical music is influenced by traditional Karelian melodies and lyrics, as comprised in the
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Kalevala" title="Kalevala">
         Kalevala
        </a>
       </i>
       . Karelian culture is perceived as the purest expression of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Finnic_peoples" title="Finnic peoples">
        Finnic
       </a>
       myths and beliefs, less influenced by
       <a href="/wiki/Germanic_peoples" title="Germanic peoples">
        Germanic
       </a>
       influence than the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nordic_folk_dance_music" title="Nordic folk dance music">
        Nordic folk dance music
       </a>
       that largely replaced the kalevaic tradition. Finnish
       <a href="/wiki/Folk_music" title="Folk music">
        folk music
       </a>
       has undergone a
       <a href="/wiki/Roots_revival" title="Roots revival">
        roots revival
       </a>
       in recent decades, and has become a part of
       <a href="/wiki/Popular_music" title="Popular music">
        popular music
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       The people of northern Finland, Sweden, and Norway, the
       <a href="/wiki/Sami_people" title="Sami people">
        Sami
       </a>
       , are known primarily for highly spiritual songs called
       <a href="/wiki/Joik" title="Joik">
        joik
       </a>
       . The same word sometimes refers to
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lavlu" title="Lavlu">
        lavlu
       </a>
       or vuelie songs, though this is technically incorrect.
      </p>
      <p>
       The first Finnish opera was written by the German-born composer
       <a href="/wiki/Fredrik_Pacius" title="Fredrik Pacius">
        Fredrik Pacius
       </a>
       in 1852. Pacius also wrote the music to the poem
       <a href="/wiki/Maamme" title="Maamme">
        <i>
         Maamme/Vårt land
        </i>
        (Our Country)
       </a>
       , Finland's
       <a href="/wiki/National_anthem" title="National anthem">
        national anthem
       </a>
       . In the 1890s Finnish nationalism based on the
       <i>
        Kalevala
       </i>
       spread, and
       <a href="/wiki/Jean_Sibelius" title="Jean Sibelius">
        Jean Sibelius
       </a>
       became famous for his vocal symphony
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Kullervo_(Sibelius)" title="Kullervo (Sibelius)">
         Kullervo
        </a>
       </i>
       . He soon received a grant to study
       <i>
        runo singers
       </i>
       in Karelia and continued his rise as the first prominent Finnish musician. In 1899 he composed
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Finlandia" title="Finlandia">
         Finlandia
        </a>
       </i>
       , which played its important role in Finland gaining independence. He remains one of Finland's most popular national figures and is a symbol of the nation.
      </p>
      <p>
       Today, Finland has a very lively classical music scene and many of Finland's important
       <a href="/wiki/List_of_Finnish_composers" title="List of Finnish composers">
        composers
       </a>
       are still alive, such as
       <a href="/wiki/Magnus_Lindberg" title="Magnus Lindberg">
        Magnus Lindberg
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Kaija_Saariaho" title="Kaija Saariaho">
        Kaija Saariaho
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Kalevi_Aho" title="Kalevi Aho">
        Kalevi Aho
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Aulis_Sallinen" title="Aulis Sallinen">
        Aulis Sallinen
       </a>
       . The composers are accompanied by a large number of great conductors such as
       <a href="/wiki/Esa-Pekka_Salonen" title="Esa-Pekka Salonen">
        Esa-Pekka Salonen
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Osmo_V%C3%A4nsk%C3%A4" title="Osmo Vänskä">
        Osmo Vänskä
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Jukka-Pekka_Saraste" title="Jukka-Pekka Saraste">
        Jukka-Pekka Saraste
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Leif_Segerstam" title="Leif Segerstam">
        Leif Segerstam
       </a>
       . Some of the internationally acclaimed Finnish classical musicians are
       <a href="/wiki/Karita_Mattila" title="Karita Mattila">
        Karita Mattila
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Soile_Isokoski" title="Soile Isokoski">
        Soile Isokoski
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Pekka_Kuusisto" title="Pekka Kuusisto">
        Pekka Kuusisto
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Olli_Mustonen" title="Olli Mustonen">
        Olli Mustonen
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Linda_Brava" title="Linda Brava">
        Linda Lampenius
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Modern">
        Modern
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Finland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Modern">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Apocalyptica1.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="900" data-file-width="600" height="255" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Apocalyptica1.jpg/170px-Apocalyptica1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Apocalyptica1.jpg/255px-Apocalyptica1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Apocalyptica1.jpg/340px-Apocalyptica1.jpg 2x" width="170"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Apocalyptica1.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Perttu_Kivilaakso" title="Perttu Kivilaakso">
          Perttu Kivilaakso
         </a>
         of
         <a href="/wiki/Apocalyptica" title="Apocalyptica">
          Apocalyptica
         </a>
         .
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       <i>
        Iskelmä
       </i>
       (coined directly from the German word
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Schlager" title="Schlager">
         Schlager
        </a>
       </i>
       , meaning "hit") is a traditional Finnish word for a light popular song. Finnish popular music also includes various kinds of
       <a href="/wiki/Dance_music" title="Dance music">
        dance music
       </a>
       ;
       <a href="/wiki/Tango_music" title="Tango music">
        tango
       </a>
       , a style of
       <a href="/wiki/Music_of_Argentina" title="Music of Argentina">
        Argentine music
       </a>
       , is also popular. The light music in Swedish-speaking areas has more influences from Sweden. Modern Finnish popular music includes a number of prominent rock bands,
       <a href="/wiki/Jazz" title="Jazz">
        jazz
       </a>
       musicians,
       <a href="/wiki/Hip_hop" title="Hip hop">
        hip hop
       </a>
       performers, dance music acts, etc.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2016)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       During the early 1960s, the first significant wave of Finnish rock groups emerged, playing
       <a href="/wiki/Instrumental_rock" title="Instrumental rock">
        instrumental rock
       </a>
       inspired by groups such as
       <a href="/wiki/The_Shadows" title="The Shadows">
        The Shadows
       </a>
       . Around 1964,
       <a href="/wiki/Beatlemania" title="Beatlemania">
        Beatlemania
       </a>
       arrived in Finland, resulting in further development of the local rock scene. During the late 1960s and 1970s, Finnish rock musicians increasingly wrote their own music instead of translating international hits into Finnish. During the decade, some
       <a href="/wiki/Progressive_rock" title="Progressive rock">
        progressive rock
       </a>
       groups such as
       <a href="/wiki/Tasavallan_Presidentti" title="Tasavallan Presidentti">
        Tasavallan Presidentti
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Wigwam_(Finnish_band)" title="Wigwam (Finnish band)">
        Wigwam
       </a>
       gained respect abroad but failed to make a commercial breakthrough outside Finland. This was also the fate of the
       <a href="/wiki/Rock_and_roll" title="Rock and roll">
        rock and roll
       </a>
       group
       <a href="/wiki/Hurriganes" title="Hurriganes">
        Hurriganes
       </a>
       . The Finnish punk scene produced some internationally acknowledged names including
       <a href="/wiki/Terveet_K%C3%A4det" title="Terveet Kädet">
        Terveet Kädet
       </a>
       in the 1980s.
       <a href="/wiki/Hanoi_Rocks" title="Hanoi Rocks">
        Hanoi Rocks
       </a>
       was a pioneering 1980s
       <a href="/wiki/Glam_rock" title="Glam rock">
        glam rock
       </a>
       act that inspired the American
       <a href="/wiki/Hard_rock" title="Hard rock">
        hard rock
       </a>
       group
       <a href="/wiki/Guns_N%27_Roses" title="Guns N' Roses">
        Guns N' Roses
       </a>
       , among others.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186">
        <a href="#cite_note-186">
         [185]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Many Finnish metal bands have gained international recognition.
       <a href="/wiki/HIM_(Finnish_band)" title="HIM (Finnish band)">
        HIM
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Nightwish" title="Nightwish">
        Nightwish
       </a>
       are some of Finland's most internationally known bands. HIM's 2005 album
       <i>
        Dark Light
       </i>
       <a href="/wiki/Music_recording_sales_certification" title="Music recording sales certification">
        went gold
       </a>
       in the United States.
       <a href="/wiki/Apocalyptica" title="Apocalyptica">
        Apocalyptica
       </a>
       are an internationally famous Finnish group who are most renowned for mixing strings-led classical music with classic heavy metal. Other well-known metal bands are
       <a href="/wiki/Amorphis" title="Amorphis">
        Amorphis
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Children_of_Bodom" title="Children of Bodom">
        Children of Bodom
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Impaled_Nazarene" title="Impaled Nazarene">
        Impaled Nazarene
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Korpiklaani" title="Korpiklaani">
        Korpiklaani
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Sentenced" title="Sentenced">
        Sentenced
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Sonata_Arctica" title="Sonata Arctica">
        Sonata Arctica
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Stratovarius" title="Stratovarius">
        Stratovarius
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Turisas" title="Turisas">
        Turisas
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Finntroll" title="Finntroll">
        Finntroll
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Ensiferum" title="Ensiferum">
        Ensiferum
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Insomnium" title="Insomnium">
        Insomnium
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Moonsorrow" title="Moonsorrow">
        Moonsorrow
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Wintersun" title="Wintersun">
        Wintersun
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Poets_of_the_Fall" title="Poets of the Fall">
        Poets of the Fall
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Waltari" title="Waltari">
        Waltari
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2016)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       After Finnish hard rock/heavy metal band
       <a href="/wiki/Lordi" title="Lordi">
        Lordi
       </a>
       won the
       <a href="/wiki/Eurovision_Song_Contest_2006" title="Eurovision Song Contest 2006">
        2006 Eurovision Song Contest
       </a>
       , Finland hosted the competition
       <a href="/wiki/Eurovision_Song_Contest_2007" title="Eurovision Song Contest 2007">
        in 2007
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187">
        <a href="#cite_note-187">
         [186]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Cinema_and_television">
        Cinema and television
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Finland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Cinema and television">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main articles:
       <a href="/wiki/Cinema_of_Finland" title="Cinema of Finland">
        Cinema of Finland
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Television_in_Finland" title="Television in Finland">
        Television in Finland
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/Lists_of_Finnish_films" title="Lists of Finnish films">
        Lists of Finnish films
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       In the film industry, notable directors include
       <a href="/wiki/Aki_Kaurism%C3%A4ki" title="Aki Kaurismäki">
        Aki Kaurismäki
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Mauritz_Stiller" title="Mauritz Stiller">
        Mauritz Stiller
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Spede_Pasanen" title="Spede Pasanen">
        Spede Pasanen
       </a>
       , and Hollywood film director and producer
       <a href="/wiki/Renny_Harlin" title="Renny Harlin">
        Renny Harlin
       </a>
       . Around twelve feature films are made each year.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Media_moves_188-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Media_moves-188">
         [187]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Finland's most internationally successful TV shows are the backpacking travel documentary series
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Madventures" title="Madventures">
         Madventures
        </a>
       </i>
       and the reality TV show
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Dudesons" title="The Dudesons">
         The Dudesons
        </a>
       </i>
       , about four childhood friends who perform stunts and play pranks on each other (in similar vein to the American TV show
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Jackass_(TV_series)" title="Jackass (TV series)">
         Jackass
        </a>
       </i>
       ).
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Media_and_communications">
        Media and communications
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Finland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Media and communications">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Linus_Torvalds.jpeg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="819" data-file-width="600" height="232" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Linus_Torvalds.jpeg/170px-Linus_Torvalds.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Linus_Torvalds.jpeg/255px-Linus_Torvalds.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Linus_Torvalds.jpeg/340px-Linus_Torvalds.jpeg 2x" width="170"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Linus_Torvalds.jpeg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Linus_Torvalds" title="Linus Torvalds">
          Linus Torvalds
         </a>
         , the Finnish
         <a href="/wiki/Software_engineering" title="Software engineering">
          software engineer
         </a>
         best known for creating the popular
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Open_source" title="Open source">
          open-source
         </a>
         <a href="/wiki/Kernel_(operating_system)" title="Kernel (operating system)">
          kernel
         </a>
         <a href="/wiki/Linux" title="Linux">
          Linux
         </a>
         .
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/Telecommunications_in_Finland" title="Telecommunications in Finland">
        Telecommunications in Finland
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/List_of_newspapers_in_Finland" title="List of newspapers in Finland">
        List of newspapers in Finland
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Thanks to its emphasis on transparency and equal rights, Finland's press has been rated the freest in the world.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189">
        <a href="#cite_note-189">
         [188]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Today, there are around 200 newspapers, 320 popular magazines, 2,100 professional magazines, 67 commercial radio stations, three
       <a href="/wiki/Digital_radio" title="Digital radio">
        digital radio
       </a>
       channels and one nationwide and five national
       <a href="/wiki/Public_broadcasting" title="Public broadcasting">
        public service radio channels
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Each year, around 12,000 book titles are published and 12 million records are sold.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Media_moves_188-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Media_moves-188">
         [187]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Sanoma" title="Sanoma">
        Sanoma
       </a>
       publishes the newspaper
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Helsingin_Sanomat" title="Helsingin Sanomat">
         Helsingin Sanomat
        </a>
       </i>
       (its circulation of 412,000
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-190">
        <a href="#cite_note-190">
         [189]
        </a>
       </sup>
       making it the largest), the
       <a href="/wiki/Tabloid_(newspaper_format)" title="Tabloid (newspaper format)">
        tabloid
       </a>
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Ilta-Sanomat" title="Ilta-Sanomat">
         Ilta-Sanomat
        </a>
       </i>
       , the commerce-oriented
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Taloussanomat" title="Taloussanomat">
         Taloussanomat
        </a>
       </i>
       and the television channel
       <a href="/wiki/Nelonen" title="Nelonen">
        Nelonen
       </a>
       . The other major publisher
       <a href="/wiki/Alma_Media" title="Alma Media">
        Alma Media
       </a>
       publishes over thirty magazines, including the newspaper
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Aamulehti" title="Aamulehti">
         Aamulehti
        </a>
       </i>
       , tabloid
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Iltalehti" title="Iltalehti">
         Iltalehti
        </a>
       </i>
       and commerce-oriented
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Kauppalehti" title="Kauppalehti">
         Kauppalehti
        </a>
       </i>
       . Worldwide, Finns, along with other Nordic peoples and the Japanese, spend the most time reading newspapers.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191">
        <a href="#cite_note-191">
         [190]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Yle, the Finnish Broadcasting Company, operates five television channels and thirteen radio channels in both national languages. Yle is funded through a mandatory television license and fees for private broadcasters. All TV channels are broadcast
       <a href="/wiki/Digital_television" title="Digital television">
        digitally
       </a>
       , both terrestrially and on cable. The commercial television channel
       <a href="/wiki/MTV3" title="MTV3">
        MTV3
       </a>
       and commercial radio channel
       <a href="/wiki/Radio_Nova_(Finland)" title="Radio Nova (Finland)">
        Radio Nova
       </a>
       are owned by Nordic Broadcasting (
       <a href="/wiki/Bonnier_Group" title="Bonnier Group">
        Bonnier
       </a>
       and Proventus Industrier).
      </p>
      <p>
       In regards to telecommunication infrastructure, Finland is the highest ranked country in the World Economic Forum's Network Readiness Index (NRI) – an indicator for determining the development level of a country's information and communication technologies. Finland ranked 1st overall in the 2014 NRI ranking, unchanged from the year before.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-192">
        <a href="#cite_note-192">
         [191]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This is shown in its penetration throughout the country's population. Around 79% of the population use the Internet.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-193">
        <a href="#cite_note-193">
         [192]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Finland had around 1.52 million broadband Internet connections by the end of June 2007 or around 287 per 1,000 inhabitants.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FICORA_194-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FICORA-194">
         [193]
        </a>
       </sup>
       All Finnish schools and public libraries have Internet connections and computers and most residents have a mobile phone.
       <a href="/wiki/Value-added_service" title="Value-added service">
        Value-added services
       </a>
       are rare.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-195">
        <a href="#cite_note-195">
         [194]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In October 2009, Finland's Ministry of Transport and Communications committed to ensuring that every person in Finland would be able to access the Internet at a minimum speed of one
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Data_rate_units#Decimal_multiples_of_bits" title="Data rate units">
        megabit-per-second
       </a>
       beginning July 2010.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196">
        <a href="#cite_note-196">
         [195]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Cuisine">
        Cuisine
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Finland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: Cuisine">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Finnish_cuisine" title="Finnish cuisine">
        Finnish cuisine
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Karjalanpiirakka-20060227.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1508" data-file-width="2048" height="162" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Karjalanpiirakka-20060227.jpg/220px-Karjalanpiirakka-20060227.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Karjalanpiirakka-20060227.jpg/330px-Karjalanpiirakka-20060227.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Karjalanpiirakka-20060227.jpg/440px-Karjalanpiirakka-20060227.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Karjalanpiirakka-20060227.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Karjalanpiirakat" title="Karjalanpiirakat">
          Karelian pasty
         </a>
         (
         <i>
          karjalanpiirakka
         </i>
         ) is a traditional Finnish dish made from a thin rye crust with a filling of rice. Butter, often mixed with boiled egg (eggbutter or munavoi), is spread over the hot pastries before eating.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Finnish cuisine is notable for generally combining traditional country fare and
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Haute_cuisine" title="Haute cuisine">
         haute cuisine
        </a>
       </i>
       with contemporary style cooking.
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fish_(food)" title="Fish (food)">
        Fish
       </a>
       and meat play a prominent role in traditional Finnish dishes from the western part of the country, while the dishes from the eastern part have traditionally included various vegetables and
       <a href="/wiki/Edible_mushroom" title="Edible mushroom">
        mushrooms
       </a>
       . Refugees from Karelia contributed to foods in eastern Finland.
      </p>
      <p>
       Finnish foods often use
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wholemeal" title="Wholemeal">
        wholemeal
       </a>
       products (
       <a href="/wiki/Rye" title="Rye">
        rye
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Barley" title="Barley">
        barley
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Oat" title="Oat">
        oats
       </a>
       ) and berries (such as
       <a href="/wiki/Bilberry" title="Bilberry">
        bilberries
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lingonberry" title="Lingonberry">
        lingonberries
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cloudberry" title="Cloudberry">
        cloudberries
       </a>
       , and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sea_buckthorn" title="Sea buckthorn">
        sea buckthorn
       </a>
       ). Milk and its derivatives like
       <a href="/wiki/Buttermilk" title="Buttermilk">
        buttermilk
       </a>
       are commonly used as food, drink, or in various recipes. Various
       <a href="/wiki/Turnip" title="Turnip">
        turnips
       </a>
       were common in traditional cooking, but were replaced with the potato after its introduction in the 18th century.
      </p>
      <p>
       According to the statistics, red meat consumption has risen, but still Finns eat less beef than many other nations, and more fish and poultry. This is mainly because of the high cost of meat in Finland.
      </p>
      <p>
       Finland is the top world consumer of coffee per capita.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-197">
        <a href="#cite_note-197">
         [196]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Public_holidays">
        Public holidays
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Finland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: Public holidays">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main articles:
       <a href="/wiki/Public_holidays_in_Finland" title="Public holidays in Finland">
        Public holidays in Finland
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Flag_days_in_Finland" title="Flag days in Finland">
        Flag days in Finland
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       All official holidays in Finland are established by Acts of Parliament. Christian holidays include Christmas, New Year's Day,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Epiphany_(Christian)" title="Epiphany (Christian)">
        Epiphany
       </a>
       , Easter,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ascension_of_Jesus_Christ" title="Ascension of Jesus Christ">
        Ascension Day
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Pentecost" title="Pentecost">
        Pentecost
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Midsummer" title="Midsummer">
        Midsummer
       </a>
       Day (St. John's Day), and
       <a href="/wiki/All_Saints%27_Day" title="All Saints' Day">
        All Saints' Day
       </a>
       , while secular holidays include May Day,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Independence_Day_of_Finland" title="Independence Day of Finland">
        Independence Day
       </a>
       , New Year's Day, and Midsummer. Christmas is the most extensively celebrated, and at least 24 to 26 December is taken as a holiday.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Sports">
        Sports
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Finland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=46" title="Edit section: Sports">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Finlandbronzecelebration2010WinterOlympics.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1280" data-file-width="1920" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Finlandbronzecelebration2010WinterOlympics.jpg/220px-Finlandbronzecelebration2010WinterOlympics.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Finlandbronzecelebration2010WinterOlympics.jpg/330px-Finlandbronzecelebration2010WinterOlympics.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Finlandbronzecelebration2010WinterOlympics.jpg/440px-Finlandbronzecelebration2010WinterOlympics.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Finlandbronzecelebration2010WinterOlympics.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Finland's
         <a href="/wiki/Finland_men%27s_national_ice_hockey_team" title="Finland men's national ice hockey team">
          men's national ice hockey team
         </a>
         is
